==PAGE:1==
Simulation of Rate-Based Distillation
Master’s Thesis
Morten Jesper Greisen Larsen - s090041
10th of July 2015
Under supervision by
Associate Professor Jens Abildskov
Associate Professor Jakob Kjøbsted Huusom
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
The theme of this thesis is to derive a model for a novel rate-based heat integrated
distillation system and determine an applicable numerical solution method to facilitate
simulations. Rate-based heat integrated distillation has been described in patents, but
a model has not been found in the literature. The concept is also not yet applied in
industry, and process simulation software such as PRO/II and Aspen do not provide
the tools to simulate such a system. The presented model and the following simulation
results are therefore the ﬁst of their kind. The concept unit is of particular interest in
processing fermentation broths from production of second generation bio-ethanol, which
is the current motivation for trying to bring the concept closer to application in industry.
It is, however, a tool like any other in the chemical engineering toolbox and might ﬁnd
use in other applications as well, once it is fully developed.
The model development is divided into the development of a model for a single sec-
tion - stripper or rectiﬁer - followed by the coupling of the sections including heat and
ﬂow integrations. The models build upon ﬂux equations describing the material and en-
ergy ﬂux across liquid-vapour interfaces, which form the basis for rate-based separation.
These ﬂux equations are adaptable to multiple components by adding more of the same
type of equations. The general scheme is not restricted to binary mixtures, but a binary
mixture of ethanol-water is the subject of this thesis.
The models were solved using a discretisation method for numerical solution, which
was executed by scripts written in MATLAB. The novelty of the model and the solution
method meant that no legacy code was available and the entire scripting was developed
during the project.
Simulation results were successfully produced for a separation task resembling the task of
concentrating the dilute ethanol product produced in a fermentation process, modelled
as ethanol-water only with no cells, proteins, or salts. Operating conditions were deter-
mined such that the purity speciﬁcations of the separation task were satisﬁed. These
results, along with a parameter study, veriﬁed qualitatively that the model is able to sim-
ulate the rate-based heat integrated distillation concept. The energy requirements using
the determined operating conditions are roughly double that of a comparable conven-
tional distillation column, but optimisation options have been identiﬁed, and the novel
unit provides other advantages that weigh it its favour. With just a few adjustments
to the operating conditions of the novel concept, closing the gap to the conventional
distillation column looks possible.
2

Preface
This Master of Science thesis represents the ﬁnal project and exam in my studies of
chemical and biochemical engineering at the Technical University of Denmark.
The
project was proposed to me by associate professors Jens Abildskov and Jakob Kjøbsted
Huusom about a year ago, and it has been a pleasure to work on a subject so per-
fectly suiting my competence and taste. The work was conducted at the research group
CAPEC-PROCSS, under the Department of Chemical and Biochemical Engineering,
from February to July 2015 and is equivalent to 35 ECTS points.
I would like to thank Jens and Jakob for good guidance and a welcoming open door
policy. Besides providing me with a fantastic project they have been supportive and
critical at the right moments, elevating the quality to a level unobtainable alone. I will
also use this opportunity to thank my beloved Stina for ever-believing support and an
almost saintlike patience; she is my muse, my ﬂame. Also thanks to my daughter, Yrsa,
who constantly deﬁes the cynicism of the surrounding world with her tenacious joyful
cheer. My friend and colleague Tobias Anker Hansen deserves a mention, for the lunch
club, a haven and a place of learning of all things related or unrelated to anything.
He has also been providing me with an extra computer to run simulations on, and has
helped with various details in LATEX. I would also like to acknowledge professor Lennart
Edsberg, the author of "Introduction to computation and modelling for diﬀerential equa-
tions", for a the very important early e-mail correspondence, without which the solution
to my models would have been terribly delayed. Lastly, I will direct a thank you to
professor Stig Wedel for sending me in Jens’ direction. It has been a very good place for
me, both personally and academically.
Morten Jesper Greisen Larsen, S090041

==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
1.1
Bioethanol
Bioethanol speciﬁcally refers to ethanol made by biological processes from biomass, in
contrast to ethanol made by chemical conversion of fossil resources. The term usually
refers to ethanol of a concentration higher than 90% by volume, but technically the
alcohol in beverages is also bioethanol.
Bioethanol is produced in large amounts in
the USA and in Brazil. These two countries, as of 2014, together accounted for 83%
of the world wide production which amounts to more than 24 billion gallons[1].
In
comparison, the world motor gasoline production in 2010 was 256 billion gallons[2]. In
the United States, bioethanol is produced by fermentation of corn, while Brazil uses
sugarcane. Bioethanol produced from these easily converted resources is referred to as
ﬁrst generation bioethanol.
Fermentation
Fermentation is cultivation and growth of micro organisms that perform some sort of task
such as production of useful proteins or simpler molecules like ethanol. Fermentation
processes are more complex than conventional chemical reactions, because the micro
organisms require many diﬀerent nutrients and salts to live and because they themselves
make the ﬂuid thick and sticky. This generally leads to diﬃculties handling fermentation
ﬂuids.
Second generation bioethanol
The fact that ﬁrst generation bioethanol uses edible crops poses a dilemma, considering
not everyone has access to enough food. It is a valid concern, and one of the major rea-
sons for the development of so-called second generation bioethanol. Second generation
bioethanol utilises biological waste material instead of what could become food. Mate-
rials like straw, hay, stalks etc. are obvious resources for second generation bioethanol
production, because the agricultural industry produces a lot of those types of material as
biproducts. These materials are indigestible for a lot of organisms, which is exactly why
they are not used to produce food. However, they still contain chemical energy. They
are of course ﬂammable, but it is desirable to have other uses and bioethanol is one such
desired product. Via ethanol, road vehicles could potentially drive on waste. Second
generation ethanol has a lot of technical obstacles, one of which is viable breakdown of
the indigestible molecules to sugars useful in fermentation. This requires the physical
structure of the feed to be broken after which enzymes can access the chemical bonds
they are designed to break. However, enzymes are an extra expenditure compared to
ﬁrst generation bioethanol.
1.2
Energy and Material Eﬃciency
It is often said that the cheapest energy is the energy you do not use. Likewise, it is
preferable to use less material to achieve the same goal and waste means that not all the
11
==PAGE:12==
1.3. NOVEL UNIT OPERATION CONCEPT
resources have been converted to a useful product.
The practical requirements for energy optimisation vary extremely by industry. Iron-
ically, the oil industry, which is often publicly perceived as dirty, messy, and careless,
is much more energy eﬃcient than most pharmaceutical productions for instance. This
is because pharmaceuticals are of very high value compared to oil based products and
as such, marginal gains in the energy budget will matter less for the total proﬁtability
of the production. Novel pharmaceuticals are also unique products that need to get to
the market as soon as possible, in order to proﬁt from a patent as long as it lasts. This
means less time spent in process design and crucial parameters such as purity matter
more than pushing eﬃciency. This wastes energy and waste materials are of limited
concern. Simple bulk chemicals, like fuel grade ethanol, are the opposite; a marginal
edge in energy or material eﬃciency could mean the diﬀerence between proﬁt and going
out of business. As a result, eﬃciency is a major topic in chemical engineering, one that
the industry and the academia has become very proﬁcient in. Novel unit operations,
control schemes, or process designs are more like to ﬁnd use in production of commodity
chemicals, where the risks of implementing something new may even be a requirement
for staying on track with competitors. In particular, material eﬃciency with regards
to the enzymes used in second generation bioethanol could be a major factor in the
cost structure. Still, eﬃciency often incur increased capital costs thus to ease investors’
minds, a compromise between eﬃciency and payback time is often required.
Waste to Value
In many processes, waste is inevitable. In these situations, it is preferable to convert
the waste to a valuable product that can be monetised instead of paying for its disposal.
Being able to make money on waste material is indubitably a good idea. Second gener-
ation bioethanol is an example of the waste to value principle and the idea is generally
gaining ground as resource scarcity and prices increase. In the bioethanol case, the waste
to value principle combines with harvesting the energy of solar rays. The sun produces
crops which are used for food, the waste material is hydrolysed by enzymes to release
the sugars used in fermentation of bioethanol.
Like many waste to value processes,
bioethanol is produced via fermentation.
Another classic example is exploiting sulphur pollutants from oil reﬁnery which the
Danish company Haldor Topsøe has been doing for more than half a century. They
produce catalysts that convert SO2 present in exhaust gas to sulphuric acid, one of the
world’s most important bulk chemicals. Since removing SO2 from the exhaust gas is a
requirement anyway, it might as well be made valuable. That is the crux of the waste
to value business model: turn the disadvantages around.
1.3
Novel Unit Operation Concept
This report revolves around a novel conceptual piece of equipment. It is a concept which
attempts to mitigate the diﬃculties of the viscosity and solid contents of fermentation
ﬂuids while also integrating the heat removed from the rectiﬁer of a distillation column
with the heat added to the stripper.
12
==PAGE:13==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Unlike the conventional distillation column, this concept has no internal trays or ﬁll-
ings, because they pose a problem with a broth liquid from a fermentation. Being able
to process fermentation broth properly could remove the capital and operating costs of
a preceding centrifuge. Even in the case there is some amount of fouling from the solids
in the fermentation liquid, the simple interior of this design makes it easy to maintain
and get back to operating condition with little downtime.
To make the heat integration between the rectiﬁer and the stripper possible, the rectiﬁer
will be operated at a higher pressure thus upgrading the energy in that region. That
way, it should be possible to transfer the heat to the stripping section. The feed enters
the cooler stripping section and, as such, the solids (including the important enzymes)
should not enter the hotter rectifying section. Therefore, operating conditions could be
set such that the enzymes are not damaged during the process. Keeping the tempera-
ture in the stripper below 65◦C should ensure the enzymes are not thermally damaged
[3], which means they can be reused in fermentation. The heat integration idea builds
upon evaporation technology and has been patented in various forms [4]. Developing a
mathematical model for this, however, has yet to be accomplished [5], and even a sim-
pliﬁed model of such a rate-based heat integrated distillation concept is an important
step towards being able to simulate and predict the performance of the novel concept.
13
==PAGE:14==
2
Background
To develop a model for the rate-based heat integrated distillation concept there are a
lot of diﬀerent technical aspects that need to be considered. This section provides an
introduction to some of the challenges and tools relevant for the work ahead. It is not
meant to provide a thorough explanation of these topics as it is assumed the reader is
familiar with the general terms and the more advanced areas will be covered in detail
later.
2.1
Chemical Engineering
The conceptual unit serves several purposes that are key in chemical engineering. As
mentioned in the introduction, it aims to reduce energy consumption by integrating the
heat removed from the rectifying section with the heat added to the stripping section.
This is accomplished by operating the rectiﬁer at a higher pressure than the stripper,
and by ensuring a way for the heat to move from high to low temperature. This is
achieved by locating one section around the other such that they share a metal wall.
The exit vapour from the top of the stripper is compressed before transfer to the bottom
of the rectiﬁer. Likewise, the bottom liquid outlet from the rectiﬁer is led through a
valve to decrease the pressure before entering the top of the stripper. This constellation
is already known as the heat integrated distillation column (HIDiC), one of several types
of advanced distillation columns resulting from process intensiﬁcation (PI) [6].
Evaporation Processes and Beyond
Evaporation, removal of solvent from a non-volatile solute, provides inspiration for heat
integrated distillation. In evaporation processes, the solvent is removed from the solute
by boiling it oﬀ. The result is a more concentrated solution. The evaporated solvent
has a high enthalpy, which can be reused in various ways. One method is vapour re-
compression where the evolved solvent vapour is compressed such that its condensation
temperature increases. The temperature gradient between the solvent vapour and the
feed is then suﬃciently high, which allows the condensation of the vapour to add to the
heat added to the feedstock. To some extent, the energy spent boiling the solvent, is
reused to boil even more solvent. This system is well suited with solutes that do not
enter the solvent vapour. In the case of a mixture of liquids, the system needs an added
rectiﬁer in order to make it a distillation system.
Patents dating back to the 1950s and 60s have presented detailed design, and in some
cases results, showing that this idea is not entirely new. Jonas Hillig compiled and de-
scribed in his bachelor thesis a list of patents that relate to the rate-based heat integrated
distillation system that we would like to explore [4]. The earliest of the listed patents is
by Eddy Eckey from 1959, ﬁled years earlier. Eckey proposes a horizontal vacuum strip-
per of highly viscous liquids that ﬂow in counter current with a stripping vapour. The
patent even mentions rectiﬁcation of the vapour following the stripping. Eckey reported
very high eﬃciency compared to the unit’s size. The design is a stripper tube inside a
14
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
hot tube such that the inner tube is heated. The inner tube also contains a rotor which
splashes the processed liquid onto the tube wall and into the vapour, facilitating heat
and mass transfer [7]. Other patents have expanded or modiﬁed or reinvented this idea
through the ages and it seems Danish inventor Erik Jensen has dedicated some thought
the concept with a patent in 1966 [8] and again in 1991 [9].
The ﬁgures 2.1 and 2.2 are an attempt to simplify and condense the designs presented in
the patents. The stripper is located in the inner tube like Eckey’s design, but a constel-
lation with the rectiﬁer in the inner tube, like Jensen’s 1966 design, is also imaginable.
Figure 2.1 shows a schematic illustration of an evaporator heat integration scheme ex-

Bioethanol Recovery
Recovering ethanol from a fermentation broth usually requires that the pseudo solid
micro organisms be removed before the ethanol is separated from what is mostly water.
These two tasks may be carried out by centrifugation followed by distillation. Conven-
tional distillation can produce the azeotropic mixture of ethanol and water; if that is
not enough the remaining water is usually removed by a molecular sieve step. One of
the features of the proposed unit is that the centrifuge should be unnecessary, because
it is capable of processing a stream with solid content. This cuts down on the number
of units which, all things equal, is very good. The fact that the process can be carried
out without a centrifuge also means that less ethanol is lost in the centrifugation step.
The cells removed in that operation are usually fairly wet such that they can be moved
16
==PAGE:17==
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND
like a ﬂuid. This means that ethanol is also present with the discarded cells.
Because bioethanol production bases itself on agricultural products (or biproducts) it
requires a lot of area of farmland.
This also means a lot of transportation.
When
considering ethanol production there are two edge solutions to the transport problem.
Either the entire production is done at one huge facility the size of which enables highly
eﬃcient energy and material integration or the entire production is carried out at each
and every little corn farm. The ﬁrst solution requires a lot of transportation, the sec-
ond solution means the small production facilities will have less integration and thereby
fewer eﬃciency options. The smaller facility with fewer integration options will beneﬁt
the most from a unit which is heat integrated with itself so this proposed unit operation
is a stepping stone towards more eﬃcient de-centralised facilities. The major advantage
of de-centralising a production like bioethanol is a reduction of the amount of material,
which is mostly water, that needs to be transported via trucks. In centralised bioethanol
production either the crops or the fermentation broth require transportation to the cen-
tral facility, but a lot of the mass of the crops or broth is not the product.
Second generation bioethanol requires the use of enzymes to break complex polysac-
charides down to simpler saccharides edible by the yeast cells. These enzymes are a
costly addition to the process so it would be favourable to recollect and reuse them.
The new unit may be able to do exactly that by operating the stripper at a pressure
below atmospheric. This will lower the operating temperature and perhaps prevent the
enzymes from denaturing [3]. How to recover them is, however, another matter.
Bioethanol is an appropriate case for the proposed unit operation, because it addresses
several issues in the recovery process. However, it is not necessarily the only application.
The unit might be worthy of consideration whenever solid content is removed prior to a
vapour-liquid separation step, or in a context where little integration with other units is
available and as such, the auto heat integration will be beneﬁcial for the energy economy.
2.2
Modelling
In contrast to conventional distillation, this concept has no trays on which the vapour
and liquid phase attain equilibrium. The separation is instead rate-based which means
that separation is limited by the rates with which material transfers from one phase
to another as the phases move. Modelling a rate-based distillation requires some con-
sideration of material and heat transfer phenomena through and between the phases.
Application of such theory is adapted from Taylor & Krishna [10]; it relies on the as-
sumption of mass and energy ﬂux continuity through the phases. These equations are
algebraic in nature. The changes in the ﬂow directions are modelled based on the princi-
ples of mass and energy conservation around control volumes in combination. The result
was a set of diﬀerential and algebraic equations.
Such combined interface ﬂux and ﬂow proﬁle models have previously been derived [11]
[4] [10] for systems consisting of one section/tube, either rectifying or stripping section,
in steady state, with symmetrical ﬂow geometries. The systems have been simulated in
co-current as well as counter current ﬂow, but in both cases the systems were formulated
as initial value problems, i.e. boundary conditions speciﬁed at one end of the tube.
17
==PAGE:18==
2.2. MODELLING
For a counter current ﬂow pattern, which will be the case in this concept, the bound-
ary conditions are inhomogeneous, meaning that diﬀerent boundary conditions are not
known at the same spatial position, unlike initial value problems that result from spec-
iﬁcations at the same position or time. It will be a step forward to be able to simulate
systems formulated as inhomogeneous boundary value problems instead of the previous
initial value problem formulation, which is too limited and not suiting for counter current
ﬂow.
18
==PAGE:19==
3
Problem Statement
The main goal of this thesis is to simulate a rate-based, heat integrated distillation sys-
tem. A mathematical model for the system will be derived and a method for solving the
model will be scripted, enabling simulations of the liquid and vapour ﬂow properties in-
side the stripper and rectiﬁer sections. Simulations attempting a "realistic" speciﬁcation
scenario will be performed in an eﬀort to verify the model, the solution method, and
the concept unit operation. Such simulations have been lacking so far, hindering further
development of this type of unit. To arrive at the simulation results the following will
be done.
A design of the system must be decided upon. At this point, a simpliﬁcation will be made,
which deviates from the patents; the orientation of the system will be vertical instead of
horizontal. This will make the ﬂows symmetrical and greatly simplify the geometrical
aspects of the model. To further simplify, the inner tube will be plain on the inside and
outside and it will be very thin. There will be no rotor or any other interior mechanisms.
The mass and energy ﬂuxes between phases will be modelled based on assumptions
of ﬂux continuity through and between the phases, and of vapour-liquid equilibrium at
an inﬁnitely thin interface. Added to that are equations describing the mass and energy
ﬂow perpendicular to the ﬂuxes, i.e. in the directions of the liquid and vapour ﬂows.
Together these equations model one section in the unit.
The model will then be solved to separately simulate the rectifying and the stripping
section of the column. This will include the streams that need to be connected later, but
these will be deﬁned rather than calculated. The cooling of the rectifying section will
be done by a simple cooling medium while the heating of the stripping section will be a
simple heating medium. At this point, it is important to consider the following coupling
and ﬁnd simulation conditions that make coupling the sections seamless.
When the separate sections are functioning they will be connected and integrated. This
entails two sorts of connections. The ﬁrst sort is the connection of the streams between
the two sections. This requires models for the compressor and the valve. The other
sort is the heat integration which is carried out by replacing the cooling and heating
mediums with temperatures from the opposing section.
The model will be used to propose a design of the unit for a realistic throughput and
purity speciﬁcations. The expectation is that this initial design might be completely in-
feasible. In that case, the consequences of the simpliﬁcations will be examined in order to
determine what their impacts are in terms of arriving at an actually useful unit operation.
It will be discussed what horizontal orientation and increased heating area pr. tube
length would mean for the mathematics and the performance of the unit.
19
==PAGE:20==
3.1. PROJECT SCHEDULE
3.1
Project Schedule
The project will be broken down into four phases representing segmented tasks that
need to be done in order to arrive at the desired goal. The ﬁrst phase is preparation and
planning. Planned hand-in is February 27th 2015.
The second phase aims to establish the foundation of the ﬁnal model by modelling
the rectifying and stripping sections in a way that makes integration doable. The issue
of counter-current ﬂow will be tackled in this phase and the mass and energy phenomena
will be reviewed. The planned hand-in date is April 15th.
During the third phase the diﬀerent model components will be connected, thus arriving
at the core purpose of this thesis. Using the techniques described previously, the model
will be solved and simulations will be presented. Solution accuracy, system stability,
and the importance of the parameters are relevant topics that will be explored during
the analysis of the model. Hand-in of this phases’ work is planned for May 29th.
The last phase is dedicated to concluding on the preceding work and rounding oﬀthe
report, making it a complete and thorough presentation of what has been achieved. This
phase is also the time where previous sections are reviewed making sure they are relevant
and coherent or changed according to modiﬁcations that may have occurred during the
project. In an ideal scenario, the bulk of the work is done and dealt with at this point,
but should any obstacles remain, hopefully, there will be time to overcome them. At the
end of this phase, the ﬁnal thesis, including all the code etc., is handed in on July 10th.

4
Distillation Concept Description
The rate-based heat integrated distillation system resides somewhere between evapora-
tion design and distillation design. Starting from the conventional distillation depicted
in ﬁgure 4.1, the changes are as follows. The conventional distillation column is heated
Stripping
section
Rectifying
section
Light product (ethanol)
Heavy product (water)
Feed
Vapour flow
Reboiler

Figure 4.1: Conventional Distillation Column
at the bottom and cooled at the top via a hot reboiled vapour inlet and a cold con-
densed reﬂux stream inlet as a liquid. The liquid ﬂowing downwards exchanges heat
with the vapour ﬂowing upwards. This new concept uses the heat integration scheme of
the HIDiC. The rectiﬁer is separated from the stripper, and the former is set to operate
at elevated pressure. Alternatively, the stripper is operated below atmospheric pressure.
If the stripper can be operated at 0.1-0.2 bar the temperature will probably be below
60◦C, which means enzymes will not denature. Either way, the rectiﬁer becomes hotter
than the stripper and heat exchange between the sections becomes possible. There are
diﬀerent ways to facilitate this heat exchange; in this concept they simply share a smooth
tube wall. Thickness and curvature of the tube wall will be neglected. At the moment,
the design is such that rectiﬁcation takes place in an outer tube while stripping takes
place inside a tube within that tube (see ﬁgure 4.2). The ﬂow patterns are similar to
the conventional distillation design, that is, the top of the stripper is connected to the
bottom of the rectiﬁer. The vapour from the top of the stripper needs compression to
get to the rectiﬁer’s operating pressure and the liquid from the bottom of the rectiﬁer
21
==PAGE:22==
Rectifier
Stripper
Figure 4.2: Concept seen from above - a tube inside a tube.
needs pressure reduction. This is shown in ﬁgure 4.3. The ﬁgure also shows a reboiler
and a condenser. These are needed for two reasons. The ﬁrst reason is the math requires
that the liquid (L) and vapour (V ) ﬂows are never zero. That means some amount of
reboil and reﬂux is needed. The second reason is that those utility units are very nice
to have for control aspects. Even though part of the motivation for this concept is to
reduce reboil and reﬂux duties, having those options is still beneﬁcial. However, it would
be preferable to have a very low reboil ratio at least, because that unit is much more
expensive than the condenser, which can use water at room temperature for cooling.
This is the HIDiC as we know it. The novelty is all of this combined with removal of
the trays. This makes the separation rate based as opposed to equilibrium based, which
calls on very diﬀerent modelling. The primary motivation for removing the trays is to
avoid fouling and ease maintenance and cleaning. As mentioned earlier, this might allow
the processing of untreated fermentation broths. The ﬁnal system is presented in ﬁgure
4.3.

5
Rate-based Distillation Section Model - Model
1
A mathematical model describing condensation of a methanol-water mixture ﬂowing
through a cooled tube is adapted from [11] (which is available in appendix H). This
model is equally useful in describing the rectifying section of the concept depicted in
ﬁgure 4.3. It may also serve to describe the stripping section, albeit with a heating
ﬂuid instead of a coolant. Changes have been made to the model, some cosmetic, some
more substantial. The updated model (termed Model 1) will be described brieﬂy in the
following sections. The changes and their reasons can be reviewed in appendix A.
The model uses ﬂux models from Taylor & Krishna [10] to describe the material and
energy ﬂuxes across the vapour-liquid interface, where thermodynamic equilibrium is
assumed. This assumption is sound because the interface does not have a volume (it
is a two-dimensional construct) and will thus attain equilibrium inﬁnitely fast. These
ﬂux equations have been adapted directly from an example in Taylor & Krishna with-
out adjustment for diﬀerence in ﬂow characteristics, because the ﬂow characteristics are
not detailed yet. However, diﬀerent ﬂow patterns, particularly turbulence, have major
impacts on how the ﬂux equations are formulated. Taylor & Krishna implement this via
correction factors to the transfer coeﬃcients (see appendix A for examples of correction
factors), with diﬀerent methods for calculating the correction factors depending on cir-
cumstances. This topic is important, but because this thesis neglects system geometry
to a large extent, it is too early to go into depth with the ﬂow modelling. Things like
turbulence simply cannot be determined at this point.
Material and energy conservation principles were used to develop diﬀerential equations
describing the changes down the tube.
The concept in this report requires counter-
current ﬂow meaning there will be inhomogeneous boundary conditions. This will be
handled in chapter 7.
5.1
System Overview
The novel concept is too much to model in one go. Figure 5.1 shows that a simpler
subsystem can be used as a starting point. The isolated subsystem coincides with the
available model, making it a simple place to start.

Figure 5.2 shows a small section of the complete system in ﬁgure 4.3. The liquid and
vapour are, in this case, located in the rectifying section with the coolant being the
liquid ﬂow in the stripping section. The two sections are separated by a tube wall. The
inlets, which are the known boundary conditions, are indicated. The same illustration
can describe the stripping section, in which case the coolant is substituted by a heating
ﬂuid. The independent variable is a, the area of the inside of the tube which increase
with the length of the tube. Length is not existing in the math. The area, a, is assumed
to be the same for the outside of the tube as well as for the contact area between the
liquid and the vapour phases. The assumption is increasingly accurate with increased
tube diameter to cylinder shell thickness ratio, but is less accurate at high liquid or tube
thickness.

5.2
Interface Fluxes
Figure 5.3 is a zoom of ﬁgure 5.2 emphasising the interface and the material and heat
ﬂuxes across it. The dashed lines indicate a transition from bulk to interface proper-
ties. It shows a distinction between liquid, interface, and vapour properties, as well as
indicating the direction of the material and energy ﬂuxes. The directions shown are the
positive directions (arbitrary deﬁnition). In the rectiﬁer, where heat is removed from
the vapour through the liquid to the cooling medium, the energy ﬂux follow the positive
ﬂux direction. The removed heat causes condensation so the total material ﬂux is also in
the positive direction, but individual component ﬂuxes may be opposite each other, as
driven by the equilibrium. The directions are generally reversed in the stripper, making
them negative in value, because the convention is not changed.
In the ﬁgure, the superscripts indicate the following regions:
C coolant, L liquid, I
interface, and V vapour. x is mole fraction in the liquid. The number of compounds
in this case is two; ethanol and water, but the modelling paradigm is designed to be
able to handle multiple compounds in the mixture, by adding more equations. xL
e is the
ethanol mole fraction in the liquid, xL
w is the water mole fraction in the liquid, xI
e is the
liquid ethanol mole fraction at the interface, and xI
w is the liquid water mole fraction
at the interface. y is the mole fraction in the vapour. yV
e is the ethanol mole fraction

The temperature variables in the ﬁgure are the bulk vapour temperature, T V , the in-
terface temperature, T I, the bulk liquid temperature, T L, and the coolant temperature,
T C. When T V > T I > T L > T C the energy ﬂux will be positive, transporting heat
energy from the vapour to the interface to the liquid and ﬁnally to the coolant.
The material ﬂux, N, is separated into a variable for each component. Ne is the ethanol
ﬂux and Nw is the water ﬂux.
The ﬂux is assumed continuous through each of the
phase, meaning that a NV (ﬂux through the vapour) is equal to NL (ﬂux through the
liquid). In other words, the ﬂux is in steady state. Therefore, there is no need for an in-
dividual ﬂux for each phase, there is just one ﬂux for each compound, ethanol and water.
The energy ﬂux has no variable, but is used in the derivation of the equations. Continu-
ity is also assumed regarding the energy ﬂux; the energy transport through the vapour
is the same as the energy transport through the tube wall to the coolant, and for any
other phase in-between.

5.5. LIQUID FLOW BALANCES

The liquid ﬂow changes down the tube area, a, only by exchanging material with the
vapour phase. This is quantiﬁed by the total ﬂux, which leads to equation 5.8. Likewise,
the component ﬂow changes by the component ﬂux. This is reformulated to a diﬀerential
equation in mole fraction instead of component ﬂow, resulting in equation 5.9. A similar
equation for the water mole fraction could be set up, but a summation equation (5.10)
is much simpler and is more certain to ensure the numerical solution method sums the
mole fractions to unity:

The liquid temperature is some function of the enthalpy exchange with the vapour as
well as the coolant. Two mechanisms exist for exchange with the vapour; conduction,

the enthalpy carried by the material ﬂux. The temperature change also depends on the
size and heat capacity of the liquid ﬂow. This all comes together in equation 5.11. All
this is described in more mathematical detail in appendix A.

The principles relevant for the liquid ﬂow apply to the vapour ﬂow as well. However,
the vapour ﬂows from down and up in the negative a-direction. This means that the

the tube. The resulting diﬀerential equation for the total vapour ﬂow (5.13) ends up
equal to the liquid ﬂow. Yet again, there is a component ﬂow equation manipulated to
yield one mole fraction and a mole fraction summation equation to yield the other.

5.9. PARAMETERS
5.9
Parameters
To solve this model a number of material properties and material and energy transfer
coeﬃcients are needed.
Heat capacities are evaluated at the indicated temperatures using empirical temper-
ature correlations. They are used without further adjustment for temperature. The
model’s derivation assumes that heat capacities are constants and that assumption is
kept for the sake of simplicity. A more explicit modelling of the heat capacities’ temper-
ature dependence should also consider the retroactive consequences, i.e. how the model’s
derivation would change, which is outside the scope of this thesis.
Transfer coeﬃcients are incredibly diﬃcult to predict without experimental data, which
of course does not exist for a conceptual piece of equipment. All k and h values are
ball-parked from typical values found in Taylor & Krishna [10]. These values are one of
the major weaknesses of this model and methods to more accurately predict said values
would be very beneﬁcial going forward in modelling these types of rate-based separation
units.

5.10
Numerical Solution Methods
The model contains both diﬀerential and algebraic equations, a so-called DAE system,
which imposes some demands on the solver. MATLAB’s suggested go-to diﬀerential equa-
tion solver, ode45, simply cannot distinguish diﬀerential and algebraic equations, consid-
ering all the provided equations diﬀerential. The more ﬂexible, but often less accurate,
ode15s is able to take a mass matrix as an argument, which is used to weight the diﬀer-
entials. A weight of 0 eﬀectively turns an equation into an algebraic equation. However,
32
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 5. RATE-BASED DISTILLATION SECTION MODEL - MODEL 1
because the boundary conditions are inhomogeneous, it is not possible to construct the
solution from one end to the other, which is what solvers such as ode45 and ode15s do.
ode15s could be used in a shooting method, a systematic way to guess values at one end
such that boundary conditions at the far end are met, but that is not what will be used
in solving the system. Instead, the plan is to discretise the equations over a grid with a
certain step size, turning the system into a massive system of algebraic equations that
should be solvable with fsolve, a non-linear equation system solver in MATLAB. Using
discretisation to handle inhomogeneous boundary conditions will be introduced via a
simple counter-current heat exchange system in the following chapter.

6
Ethanol-Water Phase Equilibria
Ethanol and water are both polar molecules and have very strong association in the
liquid phase, leading to a non-ideal liquid phase. The ethanol-water mixture is known
to form an azeotrope, which is not very dependent on pressures above atmospheric [14].
Therefore, the equilibrium will be modelled using temperature independent Margules
parameters that are determined by ﬁtting the model to Txy data at atmospheric pres-
sure. Data have been collected from two sources, as it is generally a good idea to not
depend too heavily on one source, when it comes to thermodynamic data [15] [16].
The Margules parameters have been ﬁtted to the data using a MATLAB script provided
in appendix C. The parameter ﬁt is weighted to more precisely model the equilibrium
near the azeotropic point. The ﬁtted parameters are listed in table 6.1.
Table 6.1: Fitted Margules parameters for the ethanol-water
pair
Aew = 1.6562
Awe = 0.8715
The experimental data is plotted in ﬁgures 6.1 and 6.2 along with the resulting model.
The model approximates the experimental data satisfactorily, but the lack of data at 2.5
bar is a problem. Still, 2.5 bar is not that far from atmospheric pressure, and at least
the azeotropic point is known to not depend much on pressure in that range.
The two pressures are chosen such that a suﬃcient temperature gradient between the
stripper and the rectiﬁer is virtually guaranteed at any composition. They are by no
means optimised. It would probably be more optimal to operate the stripper at low
pressure, ∼0.1 bar, and the rectiﬁer at atmospheric, but that is for another time.

7
Modelling Counter Current Systems
The models developed for the rectifying and stripping sections feature boundary condi-
tions for some variables deﬁned at one position, x1, and BCs for other variables deﬁned
at another position, x2. This is called inhomogeneous boundary conditions and can be
a bit of an obstacle for numerical solution of systems of diﬀerential equations.
A
B'
A'
B
x1
x2
Figure 7.1: General counter current problem. Boundary con-
ditions are inhomogeneous if A and B are known, while A′ and
B′ are not known.
The most common methods for numerical solutions to diﬀerential equations are some
form of construction of the solution from one point forward, from x1 to x2 for instance.
This cannot be done here.
7.1
Learning Exercise with Inhomogeneous BCs
Edsberg presents a counter current heat exchange problem [17], which will be slightly
modiﬁed and solved. The purpose is to present the solution method for a simple system
before tackling the distillation model. The example is modiﬁed in order to make it a non-
linear DAE system, like the distillation model. This makes the systems mathematically
equivalent, but the smaller system and its solution method are much easier to manage
and learn from. In ﬁgure 7.2, a hot stream enters at x = 0 at a temperature, H0, while
Figure 7.2:
System illustration of counter current heat ex-
change system
a cold stream enters at x = L at a temperature, C0. This is the foundation for the BCs
being inhomogeneous. In the equations below, H and uH are the temperature and the
ﬂow of the hot stream, respectively, with C and uC being vice versa for the cold stream.
a is a heat transfer coeﬃcient between the two chambers. The ﬂows, uH and uC, are

Discretisation Scheme
Edsberg proposes three steps of discretisation in order to break down a diﬀerential
equation problem to a system of algebraic equations [17]:
1. Discretise the x-interval to a grid
2. Discretise the diﬀerential equation
3. Discretise the boundary conditions
The x-axis is discretised in steps from x1 at x = 0 to xN at x = L. This results in a
number of N −1 x-axis pieces, which means the length of a single piece of the axis is
given by:
h =
L
N −1
The diﬀerentials are approximated by a forward ﬁnite diﬀerence approximation on the
form:
df
dx ≈f (x + h) −f (x)
h
df
dx ≈fi+1 −fi
h
for i = 1 . . . N −1
(7.2)
This transforms the diﬀerential into algebraic equations. Equation 7.2 is not applied
at i = N because that would require using fN+1 which is outside the discrete x-axis
and there is no need for solving that. At i = 1 or i = N −1, boundary conditions are
necessary, depending on the variable in question.

The cold stream has inlet at x = L and so has a BC at i = N, which comes in play in

for i = 1 . . . N
Algebraic equations each contribute N equations to the discretised system while dif-
ferential equations each contribute N −1 equations to the resulting system. Here, the
resulting number of equations is 3N −2. This means that only 3N −2 points can be eval-
uated on the discrete grid, but 3N are required to get complete solutions in all variables
across the grid. This means that 2 points must be known, i.e. the boundary conditions.
With this scheme, each variable with a diﬀerential equation must be accompanied by
one BC.
With H1 and CN as speciﬁed BCs, the solved variables are H2, H3 . . . HN, C1, C2 . . . CN−1,
and a1, a2 . . . aN. The method is very ﬂexible, as H and C can be deﬁned at any two
points, if desired.
7.2
Solving the Exercise System
The system is solved by setting up a MATLAB script that generates a vector of variables
which is sent, along with the BCs, to a function that evaluates the equations based on
40
==PAGE:41==
CHAPTER 7. MODELLING COUNTER CURRENT SYSTEMS
the variable vector. fsolve is then used to ﬁnd the solution. After the solution has been
found, the BCs are stacked with the solution to get the complete proﬁles.
The solution can be compared with a solutions generated via the MATLAB command
ode15s, which is able to solve diﬀerential algebraic equation systems with homogeneous
initial conditions (initial value problems). This is achieved by using the solution at x = 0,
found by the discretisation solution method, as "initial conditions" for the system. As-
suming the ode15s solution is fairly accurate, the diﬀerent solutions can be compared
as a way of evaluating the accuracy of the discrete solution.
Results
The script solves quickly for N = 15, but as ﬁgure 7.3 illustrates, the discrete solution
deviates from the ode15s solution, more and more as x increases. At this low value
of N, it is safe to assume that ode15s is more accurate and the deviation is the result
of the error implicit in approximating the diﬀerential quotients to ﬁnite diﬀerences.
Two accurate solutions to the same system need to be identical. Diﬀerent values of N

where tested, and at N = 70 the solutions at least look very similar. The accuracy can
be gauged quantitatively, but even in lieu of exact numbers, the impact of N is quite
evident from the diﬀerence between ﬁgures 7.3 and 7.4. For this discretisation scheme,
the error depends on the step size h in ﬁrst order. The result of this is that the step
size should be lower for a desired accuracy, compared to a scheme that depends on the
step size in the second order. This system therefore requires fairly high N to get a good
accuracy.

7.3
Discretisation of Model 1
The model presented in chapter 5 applies to the rectifying and stripping sections both,
although for the stripping section there is not a cooling medium but a heating medium,
so T C could be rewritten T H for clarity. The diﬀerent equations in the model are resolved
into F, G, or H. In the following F are algebraic equations. G are right hand sides of
diﬀerential equations of a variable with a boundary condition at a = 0; this corresponds
to ﬂow in the positive direction of a. H are right hand sides of diﬀerential equations of
variables with boundary conditions at a = 1, which corresponds to ﬂow in the negative
direction of a. In the following, the principles from the learning exercise in chapter 7
are used to discretise the distillation model into algebraic equations that can be solved
in MATLAB.
Discretisation
A discretisation scheme is used in order to reduce the diﬀerential equations to algebraic
equations. The model consists of three types of equations and also three fundamentally
diﬀerent variables. There are variables with boundary conditions at a = 0, variables
with boundary conditions at a = 1, and variables without any boundary conditions.
These variables are stacked into the Y, Z, and X vectors, respectively. The entire model
42
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 7. MODELLING COUNTER CURRENT SYSTEMS
in section 5.11 can then be written on the following general form:
F (X, Y, Z) = 0
d
daY = G (X, Y, Z)
d
daZ = H (X, Y, Z)
(7.5)

N −1
(7.6)
This scheme produces N equations for each equation of the type F, and N −1 for each
of types G and H for a total of 16N −7 equations. With a discretisation number of N,
the proﬁles can be described by N points going from a = 0 to a = A. 16N −7 points
are solved via the equations, the remaining 7 are the user deﬁned boundary conditions.
Applying the general discretisation scheme to the entire model in section 5.11 produces
the system of equations presented in the next section.
43

CHAPTER 7. MODELLING COUNTER CURRENT SYSTEMS
7.4
Programming Algorithm Description
A package of MATLAB scripts has been written in order to construct and solve the dis-
cretised Model 1. The overall concept of the algorithm is fairly simple. Parameters (p),
conditions (c), inlets (in), and discretisation number (N), are all set by the user, and the
script calls a function which produces a vector of all the variables (x0). x0 - along with
p, c, in, and N - is sent to a solver, in this case fmincon, which again calls a function
which evaluates all the equations. The solver then adjusts x0 such that the equations
ﬁnally evaluate to 0, thus solving the system. The solved x-vector is then organised in
columns for each variable with which the boundary conditions are also concatenated,
completing the proﬁles. The script produces a .txt ﬁle with the results, including the
discrete area vector in the ﬁrst column.
The entire code is available in appendix E; when reading the code referring to ﬁgure
7.5 might aid the understanding of the script. The ﬁgure shows that there is a main
script in which the user deﬁnes the system as desired. Based on these deﬁnitions, the
scripts generate an initial guess. To do that, ﬁrst the equations at the interface - equa-
tions F1 to F7 in section 5.11 - are solved at a single position. This solution is combined
with inlet speciﬁcations and stretched over the the entire tube area to be used as guess
for the proﬁles of all the variables. The script then solves the system, based on this
ﬂat proﬁle guess, for a low N value called NSimple in the code. NSimple should not be
too low; a value of around 12-14 proved useful. This solution likely has a fairly large
error (recall ﬁgure 7.3), but is a very good initial guess for an attempt at solving the
system with a high value of N. However, the number of points is not equivalent, which
is accounted for by making polynomial ﬁts of the variable proﬁles and producing a guess
with the proper number of points, N. In most cases, solving the system afterwards with
a high value of N, ∼70, with this initial guess was slow, but required few iterations. The
time it takes for the program to perform one iteration speaks to the size of the system,
while the number of iterations tells something about how close the initial guess was.
7.4.1
Choosing the solver
The obvious choice of solver is fsolve, because it is designed to solve systems of non-
linear equations, and this solver was used far along into the coding process. fsolve was
able to solve this system, but its computation time was extremely sensitive to how the
close x0 was to the solution, meaning that a lot of eﬀort went into determining x0. Math-
Works’ own online manual suggests using fmincon, a constrained minimisation tool, to
solve systems of equations [18]. This was ﬁrst introduced with Model 2, where fsolve
proved completely insuﬃcient, and the results were so good the solver was retroac-
tively ﬁtted into the previous code as well. The retroactive ﬁtting has left some eﬀorts
somewhat obsolete, like the emphasis on generating x0. Still, it has to be done somehow.
As described by MathWorks’ manual, the solver is set to minimise a function, which
for all values of x returns 0. Minimisation is then always achieved. The equations are
then set as constraints for the minimisation problem, and lower and upper bounds can
also be deﬁned to further stabilise and speed up the solution. Bounds also ensure non-
physical values for mole fractions are avoided. The solver will then attempt to minimise
the violation of the constraints i.e. solve the equation system. The increased computa-
tion speed is a convenience while working with the program, but it also directly aﬀects
47
==PAGE:48==
7.4. PROGRAMMING ALGORITHM DESCRIPTION
how high N can be set, and thus the accuracy of the solution.

==PAGE:50==
8
Simulations with Model 1
Model 1 is a stepping stone. It is used to simulate the two distillations sections to check
that it behaves as expected, and to determine boundary condition values that ease the
transition to the system with the two sections integrated. The sections are simulated
separately using the scripts listed in appendix E.
8.1
Simulating the Rectifying Section
The rectiﬁer is simulated with an operating pressure of 2.5 bar, as planned, such that
the pressure diﬀerence in the two sections ensures a driving temperature diﬀerence, no
matter the composition. The area in the simulation runs from a = 0 to a = A = 1
m2. The area is chosen such that the proﬁles are not too steep, because this makes
convergence diﬃcult, and not too ﬂat, because ﬂat proﬁles provide no information on
the system behaviour. It is also expected to not converge at all at too high area, because
the model cannot handle one of the phases disappearing completely.
While simulating the rectiﬁer and the stripper, the boundary conditions of each were
manually tweaked in an attempt to make the proﬁles connect. This was done by trial and
error, using results from one simulation as inlets for the other and back again, because
of the way inhomogeneous BCs make such iterations troublesome. The purpose was to
simulate the rectiﬁer and the stripper separately, but in a way that their results were
compatible, a kind of pseudo integrated system. The purpose is to ﬁnd inlets that are
realistic for both sections, in preparation to their upcoming integration. Perfecting this
would have required a lot of time or some clever programming, which also takes time.
The eﬀort resulted in the set inlet values for the rectiﬁer listed in table 8.1 (T L and T V
are approximates of their computed bubble point and dew point, respectively).
Table 8.1: BCs for Model 1 simulation of the rectifying section
L (0) = 4 mol

decently with the desired pseudo integration.

Figure 8.5 shows the temperatures of the liquid and vapour phases and of the coolant.
The vapour is predictably cooled from right to left, but the liquid temperature proﬁle
is more complicated. It drops steeply from a = 0 to 0.2 m2, then increases in a more
steady fashion, almost in parallel with the coolant temperature. This is likely the result
of a very high h0, which aﬀects the heat transfer across the tube wall.
The liquid
temperature is thus much more dependent on the coolant temperature than on the
vapour temperature. Also, the liquid inlet temperature may be too high compared to
the coolant temperature, but the coolant temperature is tweaked to be in the region of
T L in the stripper simulations. This may be the result of an unnecessarily high pressure
diﬀerence between the sections.

Lastly, the ﬂuxes across the interface are plotted, component wise and total ﬂux (ﬁgure
8.6). Referring to ﬁgure 5.3, positive values of ﬂux indicate transport from the vapour
to the liquid phase. The proﬁles indicate that the separation is very eﬀective, in that
the ethanol is going from the liquid to the vapour phase, and the water is going in the
opposite. Seeing as the tube is cooled, it is somewhat surprising that the ethanol has
negative ﬂux. This must be due to a fairly high ethanol content in the liquid compared
to the vapour, and thus a high driving force towards equilibrium, but optimism should
be tempered as the inlet ﬂows are not completely realistic yet. The total ﬂux is positive
almost along the entire length of the tube, verifying that, overall, the vapour condenses.
The slight dip below 0 near a = 0 seems to be another result of the maybe too hot liquid
inlet temperature, and is inconsequential to the result as a whole.
Parameter Testing
A number of parameters and inlet values were tested. To do that, their eﬀect on the
product - that is the ethanol composition and vapour ﬂow rate at the top - were calcu-
lated with variations to said values.
Material and energy transfer coeﬃcients are notoriously diﬃcult to estimate, and as
such, it is important to have an idea of the solution’s sensitivity to those. Pressure is
part design and part operating condition that may be used as part of a control scheme.
Tube area is the most obvious design parameter to use in order to obtain the desired
product composition. The inlet conditions have some association to reﬂux and reboil
ratios and also to feed composition and ﬂow rate. This association is very loose in Model
1, as they are currently completely independent parameters.
56
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 8. SIMULATIONS WITH MODEL 1

at the top. Each parameter was increased by 50% and decreased by 50% and the prod-
uct variables (V (0) and yV
e (0)) were calculated and presented as a measure of the system
sensitivity.

Of the transfer coeﬃcients, only one seems to have much impact on the solution: kV ,
the material transfer coeﬃcient in the vapour phase, at least with the current estimated
coeﬃcients.
Increasing kV , predictably, increases product purity.
It is a little more
surprising that product ﬂow rate is also increased, but as ﬁgure 8.6 shows, the interface
ﬂuxes are a bit complicated. Pressure has a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the solution, determining
temperatures and vapour density which has inﬂuence on the interface ﬂuxes. Pressure
might indeed be a reasonable tool in a control scheme. A larger area, of course, increases
product purity while decreasing product ﬂow rate, but not by all that much due to the
negative ethanol ﬂux.
V (A) is some measure of the reboil ratio, which might also
be a manipulated variable. Having more vapour, with lower ethanol content than the
product, coming into the system reduces the product purity, while increasing the amount
of product, as expected. If the inlet vapour contains more ethanol the product is purer,
while the ethanol ﬂux is decreased, due to lower diﬀerent in mole fraction between the
57
==PAGE:58==
8.2. SIMULATING THE STRIPPER
liquid and the vapour phase. The result is slightly less product ﬂow. Increasing T C
makes the system a stripping section, which evaporates the liquid, forcing more material
to the vapour phase and product ﬂow rate. Having a stripper at the top is obviously bad
for the product purity. Conversely, making the coolant colder slightly increases purity,
while reducing product ﬂow rate. Increasing the amount of coolant has very little eﬀect
altogether. L (0) has some connection with a future top reﬂux ratio. Its eﬀects on the
product correspond to this. Lastly, feeding a liquid richer in ethanol (higher xL
e ), makes
the product purer.
8.2
Simulating the Stripper
The stripper has also been studied, but since both sections apply Model 1, there is little
reason to describe both sections in detail. The system is mathematically equivalent to
the rectiﬁer, and ﬁgure 8.7 shows the diﬀerence comes down to inlet speciﬁcations and
operating conditions. The stripper applies a heating medium instead of a coolant. It is,
as such, an evaporator. The following a short presentation of the simulation results of
the stripping section.
The operating pressure in the stripper is 1 atm.
The area variable, a, runs from 0
at the top to 1 at the bottom.
Table 8.3: BCs for Model 1 simulation of the stripping section
L (0) = 8 mol
s
xL
e (0) = 0.48
T L (0) ≈80◦C

vapour (negative ﬂux) while the water moves from the vapour to the liquid (positive
ﬂux). The total ﬂux is negative, which means that overall material is moving from the
liquid to the vapour, i.e. evaporation.

==PAGE:65==
9
Concluding on Model 1
The developed Model 1 has been solved with inhomogeneous boundary conditions using
a discretisation method to convert the diﬀerential-algebraic equation system to one of
only algebraic equations, which could be solved using MATLAB’s fmincon minimisation
function with constraints. Simulations have shown that the results from Model 1 are
plausible and changes in diﬀerent parameters aﬀect the results as foreseen.
It has been mentioned (page 40), but not yet shown, that the boundary conditions
can in fact be speciﬁed at any spatial position in the system, because the entire system
is solved simultaneously in the minimisation algorithm. These boundary conditions may
also be deﬁned by additional equations. This enables studies of new geometries such as
that required by the coupled distillation system (see ﬁgure 4.3).
In the following chapters, Model 1 will be the foundation for Model 2, resembling better
the actual concept on page 23. This will be done by connecting the streams from and to
the rectiﬁer and stripper sections, as well as replacing the variable T C with the liquid
temperature in the opposing section, with which heat is exchanged. There will basically
be one Model 1 for the rectiﬁer and one Model 1 for the stripper, as well as modelling
of the compressor, the valve, the reﬂux, and the reboil streams. There will also be an
actual feed ﬂow, and the diﬀerent inlet ﬂows speciﬁed in Model 1 are instead computed
via the compressor, valve, reﬂux, and reboil equations.
65
==PAGE:66==
10
Heat Integrated System Model - Model 2
The aim is to expand upon Model 1 to enable a simulation of the rate-based heat inte-
grated distillation concept. Figure 10.1 describes the logic in coupling the two sections.
Consider ﬁrst the simulation of the two uncoupled sections (upper left corner). This is
straight forward using a single Model 1 for the stripper and another Model 1 for the
rectiﬁer. This means setting up one set of equations where all variables are tagged with
an S for the stripper and another set of equations with the variables tagged with an R
for the rectiﬁer (and adjusting all other condition dependencies accordingly). These can
be solved simultaneously or consecutively (as in chapter 8) giving identical results.
As the coloured liquids in ﬁgure 10.1 indicate, the stripper liquid ﬂow and the rectiﬁer
coolant ﬂow are the same. Vice versa for the stripper heating ﬂuid ﬂow and the rectiﬁer
liquid ﬂow. The ﬁrst step then, is removing the utility streams. Mathematically, this
is done by removing equation G4 from the section 5.11 Complete Model 1. Also, all
T C,S are replaced by T L,R, and T C,R are replaced by T L,S wherever these appear in the
model. The result of this is a model, with a solution resembling the conﬁguration in the
lower right corner of ﬁgure 10.1.
The sections are now heat integrated, and the next step illustrates the material inte-
gration needed to simulate the ﬁnal concept. The mathematics of the last step includes
setting up the algebraic equations that make compositions and ﬂow rates of the connected
streams equal, but also equations that adjust pressures and temperatures. Lastly, the
reﬂux and reboil streams (red and blue dashed lines) are some determined ratios of the
exiting vapour (top of rectiﬁer) and liquid (bottom of stripper).
Summing up, the steps are as follows:
1. Set up two models, indicating variables with S and R.
2. Heat integration. Remove the coolant and heating ﬂuid. Delete equations GS
4 and
GR
4 . T C,S →T L,R and T C,R →T L,S.
3. Material integration. Introduce compressor, valve, reﬂux, and reboil.
The entire mathematical parallel to ﬁgure 10.1 can be read in appendix B. The following
sections present the less trivial (i.e. new) excerpts of that appendix.

==PAGE:68==
10.1. COMPRESSOR
10.1
Compressor
The vapour from the top of the stripper is compressed before it is fed into the bottom
of the rectiﬁer. This compression is simpliﬁed to the following set of equations, where
energy is neglected in terms of proper modelling. The outlet temperature is calculated to
the dew point temperature (Td) at those conditions, which can be achieved with cooling
or heating, even if it is not the wisest thing to do. This is a point that could be handled
more rigorously, but this simpliﬁcation made convergence easier.
C1 ≡yV,R
e
(A) −yV,S
e
(0) = 0
(10.1)
C2 ≡V R (A) −V S (0) = 0
(10.2)
C3 ≡T V,R (A) −Td

yV,S
e
,Ae,Aw,P R
= 0
(10.3)
Equation 10.3 is a procedure of MATLAB functions rather than a mathematical equation.
Td is a function for the dew point temperature, which is dependent on the composition,
the pressure, and the thermodynamic model, which again is Margules’ activity using
the parameters Ae and Aw ﬁtted to data in chapter 6. It serves the purpose of setting
the output from the compressor to the dew point temperature. Using Td is somewhat
arbitrary, but it was a simple decision to start from. It is quite likely that this requires
cooling, in which case it seems a very unoptimised design, seeing as the point of the
pressure increase is to achieve higher temperature. It is a point that is very relevant in
future optimisation of the concept.
10.2
Valve
The valve is handled similarly to the compressor, although the feed stream is handled in
the same equations, even though that would practically take place in a separate mixing
unit. The ﬂow rate of the feed is assumed constant (F) as is its ethanol content (xF
e ). The
temperature from the valve is set to the bubble point temperature, again for simplicity.
Equation 10.4 is an ethanol balance and equation 10.5 is a total material balance.
V1 ≡xL,S
e
(0) −xF
e F + xL,R
e
(A) LR (A)
LS (0)
= 0
(10.4)
V2 ≡LR (A) + F −LS (0) = 0
(10.5)
V3 ≡T L,S (0) −Tb

xL,S
e
(0) , Ae, Aw, P S
= 0
(10.6)
Like equation 10.3, equation 10.6 is a procedure which determines the bubble point
temperature of the liquid coming from the valve and mixed with the feed. An isenthalpic
pressure reduction would likely outlet a stream in the two-phase region. Adding such a
two-phase stream to the top of the stripper would raise the question of what happens
to the vapour fraction. Using the bubble point temperature circumvents that issue, for
now. This means, there is wasted heat which is simply not accounted for in the valve
equations. This wasted heat may be utilised to pre-heat the feed, with which the hot
valve outlet is mixed. However, this possibility depends of the feed temperature and
whether it even needs pre-heating. At any rate, a more detailed model of the valve,
including energy considerations, is a welcome addition to the model, at some later point.
68
==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 10. HEAT INTEGRATED SYSTEM MODEL - MODEL 2
10.3
Top Reﬂux
The vapour outlet from the top of the rectiﬁer is split into a product stream and a reﬂux
stream which is cooled in a condenser. The amount of material reﬂuxed is the product
ﬂow rate times the reﬂux ratio, TRR:
Prodtop · TRR = LR (0)
The product ﬂow rate is not an existing variable, so this relationship is manipulated a
little to get equation 10.7. The ethanol contents are of course equal, in and out of the
condenser. The temperature is set to the bubble point as with the valve.
Reflux1 ≡LR (0) + LR (0)
TRR −V R (0) = 0
(10.7)
Reflux2 ≡yV,R

(0) = 0
(10.8)

= 0
(10.9)
10.4
Bottom Reboil
Some of the bottoms product is likewise reboiled, sending a fraction back and the re-
maining out as product. The same principles apply. The temperature is the dew point,
BRR is the bottom reboil ratio.
Reboil1 ≡V S (A) + V S (A)
BRR −LS (A) = 0
(10.10)
Reboil2 ≡yV,S

= 0
(10.12)
At this point, it should be noted that the duties for the condenser, reboiler, compressor,
and the implicit heat adjustments in the compressor and valve equations are not mod-
elled. These will later be determined by extracting composition, temperature, pressure,
and phase fraction (which is always 1 or 0 in this model) from the simulation results,
and simulating corresponding unit operations in the process simulation software PRO/II.
PRO/II can then calculate the duties.
10.5
Number of Equations and Variables
The number of equations and variables in 2 times the discrete Model 1 is 2×(9N + 7 (N −1))
(from originally 9 algebraic and 7 diﬀerential equations). Model 2, in comparison, re-
moves 2 × (N −1) equations in the G4,R and G4,S equations, and adds 12 equations for
the compressor, valve, reﬂux, and reboil.
2 × (9N + 7 (N −1)) −2 × (N −1) + 12 = 30N
Model 2 has 30N equations and variables. The added equations have eﬀectively made
all the variables equal when it comes to having BCs or not. In Model 1, the proﬁles from
69
==PAGE:70==
10.6. OPERATING CONDITIONS
the diﬀerential equations had to have the BCs concatenated to their solutions, but this
is no longer the case, as the entire length of the proﬁles are unknown variables. This
makes the system neatly symmetric.
10.6
Operating Conditions
Model 2 is simpliﬁed in one aspect compared to using two times Model 1, namely inputs.
Model 2 needs fewer boundary conditions speciﬁed, because a lot more is now connected.
The conditions that can be tweaked more closely resemble realistic design and operation
parameters. These are total phase contact and heat integration area (which are simpliﬁed
to be the same variable), the two pressures, the reﬂux, and reboil ratio. Those are the
tools available for separating a feed ﬂow with a given ethanol content.

11
Solving Model 2
Solving Model 2 is similar to solving Model 1 in many ways, and diﬀerent in a few.
The initial guess was not generated with a low N, but via a range of diﬀerent methods
depending on what results have been generated so far. In other words, the capabilities of
the simulation scripts are high dependent on previously generated data ﬁles. The main
ﬁle, mainModel2.m, available in appendix F has a number of options for initial guess
generation.
The simplest is useFlatGuessFromFile, which loads a ﬁle with 30 values in it, one
for each variable. These are then stretched to the chosen value of N, thus constructing
the initial guess vector, x0. The solver will use x0 and attempt to converge. This may
create problems if the values in the guess ﬁle are bad or if the proﬁles are too steep,
but this can be helped along by choosing a low area, A, such that the proﬁles become
ﬂatter (as nothing much will happen in a short tube). Generally, the solver has been
seen to solve simple systems with equimolar feed, reﬂux and reboil ratios at 1, and low
area using guesses that were rather rough estimates.
Once convergence has been attained things become much easier, because that result
is saved and can be used as a complete guess using the option haveFullGuess (this
overrides useFlatGuessFromFile). Also, the system can be changed gradually by using
the Variation vectors to increment one variable. This is handy when the desired area
is 50 m2, but it was not possible to solve with more than 1 m2. The areaVariation
can then be set to increment from 1 to 50 by steps of 1, for instance. Each solution is
automatically saved and used as a guess for the next iteration. The last result can then
be used as the guess for a new set of increments on another of the operating conditions
listed in table 10.1, hopefully, reaching a result for the desired simulation speciﬁcations.
A third option relates to the initial guess generation. adjustN is an addition to the
option haveFullGuess and converts the results in the provided ﬁle to a set correspond-
ing to a diﬀerent value of N using polynomial ﬁts. N is an important factor, because not
only does it determine the accuracy of the solution, its value is also critical for converge.
Particularly at low values of mole fractions, which are of course relevant for high purity
separations, a high value of N (80 or even higher in some cases) proved necessary. Very
low values of N (< 14) often could not solve even simple systems. Some middle ground
(20 −30) was useful in moving the system using the incrementing loops without taking
too long to compute. Generally, whatever value of N makes the system small and still
converging, is good for speed and getting the ﬁrst set of results that can then be built
upon.
The discretisation and actual solving of Model 2 was accomplished in a fashion simi-
lar to solving Model 1 using fmincon. However, the calculation time was much longer.
The slowest successful simulation took more than 13 hours. Many attempted simulation
ran for longer, but needed to be cancelled as the solver appeared to be going nowhere.
Some sets of desired simulation speciﬁcations are yet to be produced because it is very
75
==PAGE:76==
11.1. EXAMPLE OF DIVERGENT SOLUTION
diﬃcult to predict what methods will result in successful convergence or a waste of time.
All primary results have been achieved, but particularly studying the eﬀect of changing
the top reﬂux ratio proved too diﬃcult at this point.
11.1
Example of Divergent Solution
Many simulations have been made before ﬁnding presentable solutions. Below are the
results of a simulation run with the same inputs as those presented in chapter 12, except
for N, which is much lower. At the time, it seemed that whenever the mole fractions
looked reasonable, the rest would also be plausible. It became evident, however, that
some variables may behave reasonable, while others do not. Figure 11.1 shows that the
mole fractions behave reasonably.

Figure 11.1: Mole fractions.
This is one of a handful of simulations that took more than 10 hours, and the explanation
appears to be that the entire solution is not as smooth as ﬁgure 11.1 indicates. The
variables T L, Ne, and Nw diverge (ﬁgures 11.2 and 11.3) because the discretisation is
too coarse, but it also seems the temperatures from the top reﬂux and the valve are
not entirely practical. The liquid from the top reﬂux is fairly hot, while the liquid from
the valve is cold. These two streams are in thermal contact with each other at a = 0
m2 and exchange energy. This seems to happen at a rate where the hot liquid in the
rectiﬁer drops dramatically in temperature while the temperature of the colder liquid in
the stripper increases at a similarly dramatic rate. These very steep slopes require high
accuracy (high N) to solve and a failure to meet the required accuracy results in the
following ﬁgures 11.2 and 11.3. The next chapter will discuss the successful results in
more detail, the presentation here serves to illustrate the failed results.

==PAGE:78==
12
Simulations with Model 2
To really test the usefulness of Model 2, a "realistic" separation task is deﬁned. It is
realistic in the sense that the mole fractions are set to resemble what can be expected
in bioethanol reﬁnery. The inlet feed has a low ethanol content of 3 mol%, which is
around the strength of a weak wine. This needs to be satisfactorily concentrated, which
means the top product must be brought close to the azeotrope of ∼86 mol% ethanol,
while producing fairly pure water as bottoms product. The feed rate is quite low, at 100
mol/s. Industrial scale productions are easily a hundred fold larger, but if the next step
in the development of this concept is a pilot plant, this may be in the right magnitude.
In table 12.1, the pressures are already decided upon because they a ensure temper-
ature driving force between the sections. The feed ﬂow rate and its ethanol content are
also set. This leaves A, TRR, and BRR as the tuning inputs available for achieving the
desired purities. BRR was not used much, as changes to this input proved problematic
for the solver for some reason. A and TRR were increased until some combination pro-
duced a satisfactory ethanol purity in the top product. Water purity was not a concern
as it was fairly high in most simulations, likely due to the locked BRR, and the high
amount of water in the feed.
Table 12.1: List of inputs for simulation of Model 2
A = 300 m2
P R = 2.5 bar
P S = 1 atm.
N = 200
TRR = 30
BRR = 1
F = 100 mol
s
xF
e = 0.03
There is still a way to go before a design procedure can be formulated. As such, many
simulations came before those presented below. As described in chapter 11, the solution
was gradually moved one input parameter at a time. The set of inputs in table 12.1
did not solve satisfactorily even at N = 140 where some variables still oscillated. The
minimiser did converge to a very low error, but the results were not physically plausible.
This problem became increasingly pronounced at higher values of maximum area, which
is unsurprising because when area is increased without increasing N, the absolute step
size increases, making the approximation less accurate.
At N = 200, 6 GB of RAM was insuﬃcient, but another computer with 16 GB of
RAM was able to solve and provide the following results.
The product properties and production rates from the simulation are given in table
12.2. The ﬂow rate of the light product is very low, but the system is only fed with 3
mol/s ethanol so it makes sense. The ethanol content of the tops product is close to
the azeotrope. It may be possible to get even closer with higher A or reﬂux ratio. A
realistic demand on the water purity is probably not met, but without a good design
procedure this will have to do. Note that the material balance closes fairly, considering
the numerical nature of a discretisation method.

The following plots are a little diﬀerent from those presented in chapter 8 because both

the rectiﬁer, while the area from 300 to 600 m2 represents the stripper. The far left,
a = 0 m2, is then the top of the rectiﬁer, where the top product exits, and the far right,
a = 600 m2, is the bottom of the stripper, where the bottom product exits. Although
the stripper is thought to be inside the rectiﬁer, it is useful to think of the area going
through the rectiﬁer and then through the stripper. This resembles going downwards in
a conventional column, because the proﬁles develop like they would if the sections were
arranged in succession.

Figure 12.1: Total molar ﬂow rates from simulation of Model
2. Area from 0 to 300 m2 is the rectiﬁer, and from 300 to 600 m2
is the stripper. The liquid ﬂows downwards (to the right) and
the vapour ﬂows upwards (to the left). The large step in liquid
ﬂow rate is due the addition of the feed, which is all in the liquid
phase.
Figure 12.1 illustrates the liquid and vapour ﬂow proﬁles through the rectiﬁer followed by
the stripper. In the rectiﬁer, the vapour condenses and it decreases in its ﬂow direction
(up/to the left), while the liquid ﬂow increases in its ﬂow direction (down/to the right).
The stripper section is opposite because evaporation takes place, in accordance with
expectations. The feed is added in the top of the stripper (a = 300) which means the
liquid ﬂow rate makes are large step. The vapour from the top of the stripper (a = 300)
is added to the bottom of the rectiﬁer (a = 300) via the compressor, without any change
to its molar ﬂow rate.
These types of ﬂow proﬁles, where the ﬂows are large near the feed and then decline
going away from the feeding point, are also what is seen in the more established HIDiC
type of distillation.

The results of the component molar ﬂow rates are presented in ﬁgure 12.2. The ethanol
in liquid and ethanol in vapour proﬁles show an interesting behaviour in the rectiﬁer. In
the direction of the vapour ﬂow, those two proﬁles are increasing from around a = 300
to a = 200 m2 and decreasing from a = 200 to a = 0 m2. In the ﬁrst region the ﬂuxes are
favourable in the way that the purity of the vapour increases (because water in vapour
also drops in that region) and the production rate - measured in terms of ethanol, not
total product - also increases. In the second region (200 to 0 m2), the production rate
is sacriﬁced for product purity, which is more evident in ﬁgure 12.3. The feed adds are
large bump to the water in liquid, and a tiny addition to the ethanol in liquid.

The mole fractions for the liquid and vapour phases presented in ﬁgure 12.3 show that
the compositions of the liquid and vapour follow each other very closely in the rectiﬁer,
while the addition of the feed disturbs this tendency in the stripper. Proﬁles are generally
steeper in the stripper, which can be understood by recalling ﬁgure 6.1, which shows the
phase envelope is much larger closer to the pure water end of the phase diagram. This
provides more driving force towards equilibrium and thus steeper proﬁles of the mole
fractions. Qualitatively, it is evident that the water produced from the bottom of the
stripper is fairly pure. The ethanol is not as pure because it is limited by the azeotrope,
but it does land comfortably close to the azeotrope which the thermodynamic model
calculates to ∼86 mol%.
