Figure 12.4: Temperatures from simulation of Model 2. T L
has signiﬁcant, steep jumps at a = 0 m2 and a = 300 m2, which
are taxing on the discretisation accuracy. Very high N is needed
to solve that steepness correctly.
Figure 12.4 shows the simulated temperature proﬁles. The steepness of T L in the recti-
ﬁer (near a = 0) is the result of a high temperature coming from the reﬂux, while the
jump at a = 300 is the result of the low temperature coming from the valve. These
two areas are thermally connected, which means they are actually the same problem
manifesting at diﬀerent positions in the plot. The issue then seems to be that the tem-
perature diﬀerence is simply too high. The most immediate solution to this problem is
to reduce the pressure diﬀerence between the sections, something that would also reduce
the duty of the compressor. It is not surprising that the pressure diﬀerence is too high;
it was deliberately set suﬃciently high. At the moment, this seems to be the source of
a lot of computation related issues.
The vapour temperature at the bottom of the stripper (a = 500 . . . 600 m2) is higher
than the liquid temperature, but this changes further up the stripper (a = 300 . . . 500
m2). The reason is that the liquid is being heated by the rectiﬁer, while the vapour is
not. It also means that at the bottom of the stripper the vapour heats the liquid to
a temperature higher than the the liquid in the rectiﬁer, almost inverting the thermal
driving force.
The proﬁle for the total material ﬂux in ﬁgure 12.5 actually becomes
slightly positive (condensation) at the very end of the stripper.

Figure 12.5: Fluxes from simulation of Model 2. Ne still has

Looking at ﬁgure 12.5, the total ﬂuxes look fairly straight forward. The rectiﬁer features
positive total ﬂux, meaning overall condensation, and the stripper features negative ﬂux,
overall evaporation (except at the bottom). However, the component ﬂuxes are very in-
teresting. In large sections of the whole unit they have inverted signs, which is very
beneﬁcial for the separation process. The area from 200 to 300 m2 supports what was
observed in ﬁgure 12.2: the ethanol ﬂux is negative, it moves from the liquid to the
vapour, which results in overall more ethanol in the product and also purer product
going from 300 to 200 m2. From 200 to 0 m2, the ethanol ﬂux become positive, and
the amount of ethanol is sacriﬁced for the purity of it. The stripper displays a similar
tendency, only inverted, in which the overall transport is from liquid to vapour, but
the water moves from the vapour to the liquid. This serves to support the very idea of
rate-based separation.
The results are generally very nice, but some steep sections of the T L proﬁle (ﬁgure
12.4) seem to still be a source of some issues which also aﬀect the ﬂuxes (ﬁgure 12.5).
Moving forward, it would be a good idea to adjust T L at the inlets to temperatures that
ease the solution. Waiting several hours for one simulation is a major issue.
12.1
Key streams properties
In order to evaluate the performance of the separation in terms of utility requirements,
purity, production rate, and equipment size, the following streams are important. Ob-
viously, the feed and the two product streams are important, but the internal streams
of reﬂux, reboil, compressor, and valve in- and outlets are also needed to evaluate what
sort of duties are required of those utility units.

sults). The product ﬂow rates are also not given by the simulation results but can be
found via the rectiﬁer vapour ﬂow at the top and the reﬂux ratio and likewise for the

up slightly diﬀerently from how they are depicted in the concept illustration (ﬁgure 4.3),
because the valve and the compressor are actually modelled with implicit temperature
regulation, i.e. heat exchangers. This was to facilitate solution of Model 2. These heat
exchangers are added in the PRO/II simulations, as seen on ﬁgures 12.6b and 12.6a.

The cooling duties can be handled using cooling water at room temperature, which is
relatively inexpensive. The heating duty in the reboiler is more expensive, but it is not
extremely hot and can be handled with steam at moderate pressure. The compressor
requires electricity which can be expensive depending on the supply at or near the site.
The most expensive unit could be the reboiler, another incentive to reduce the bottom
reboil ratio along with the fouling considerations (burning of cells or proteins). The
compressor might also be quite expensive, which begs the examination of the required
pressure diﬀerence. There are indications that a diﬀerence of 1.5 bar is needlessly high.

==PAGE:88==
12.2. UTILITY DUTIES
Conventional Distillation Comparison
The same separation task has been simulated in PRO/II for a conventional distillation
column. Reiterating, the task was to take a feed of 3 mol/s ethanol mixed with 97 mol/s
water at 80.57◦C and 1 atm. and produce a top product with 83.44 mol% ethanol and
a bottom product with 99.70% water. In both cases, the top product is not condensed.
The number of trays for this column was chosen as a compromise between operating
costs and capital cost. 10 trays yielded much lower condenser and reboiler duties than
9 trays, and although 11 trays yielded a further reduction it was less signiﬁcant and 10
trays was chosen as the cut-oﬀpoint. The feed location was optimised to yield the lowest
reboiler duty via a PRO/II optimisation tool, which basically goes through all 10 trays
as feed location and ﬁnds the lowest duty. The optimal feed location is 9th tray, which
corresponds with the very low ethanol content.
Figure 12.8
The simulation was successful and the duties were provided and the reﬂux and reboil
ratios can be computed from the PRO/II generated report given in appendix G. The
duties are listed in table 12.5. The top reﬂux ratio was 15.97 and the bottom reboil ratio
0.5747.
Table 12.5: Utility duties in conventional distillation column
Unit
Duty [MW]
Temperature [◦C]
CONDENSER
-2.03
78.15
REBOILER
2.30
99.15
The duties in the conventional column are about half of the duties required for the novel
concept. Here one must consider that the novel concept has not been optimised at all
88
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 12. SIMULATIONS WITH MODEL 2
while the conventional column is roughly optimised with regards to the number of trays
and the feed location. Model 2 could be modiﬁed to handle a diﬀerent feed location,
as the ﬁrst step in trying to reduce the condenser and boiler duties. Finding a balance
between area and relfux/reboil ratios is a more complicated balance between capital and
operating costs. It also would beneﬁt from expansion upon the model with inclusion
of the geometry, potential column packing, and some solution to the division-by-zero
problem in equations 5.9 and 5.11, so that the system can be simulated with zero, or
near-zero, reboil.
The sizes are diﬃcult to compare as the geometry of the novel concept is still unde-
ﬁned, and packing material - which is an option in the rectiﬁer as no solid material
is present - can produce a very large contact area in very little space. For instance,
small Intalox saddles provide 623 m2/m3, which is the highest area pr. volume in a list
of packings in McCabe-Smith-Harriott [19]. When the characterisation of the concept
unit becomes more mature, a comparison of their sizes and capital costs will make more
sense.
12.3
Parameter Alterations
Model 2 has been simulated with variations to a few parameters to get an idea of their
inﬂuence. Table 8.2 suggests that kV is the transfer coeﬃcient which aﬀects the results
the most, so this will be studied, now for Model 2. The pressure has been mentioned in
connection with a possible control paradigm, so that is also tested. Of course, Model 2
cannot provide the dynamics of a step change, but the change in steady state solution is
an indication of the behaviour. It may also be interesting to examine the consequences
of a lowered pressure in the rectiﬁer as the compressor duty is potentially costly. The
top reﬂux ratio might also be involved in control and general design so this was also to
be tested, but convergence was unobtainable at the moment and the endeavour had to
be dropped.
The system will be simulated using a ±50% change in the variables, kV and P R, one
change at a time. All other inputs and parameters will have the same values as a base
case. The base case is the solution presented earlier in this chapter (chapter 12). The
expectation is that a reduced kV should reduce the ﬂuxes across the interface and thus
the degree of separation, while increasing kV should increase the degree of separation,
limited of course by the azeotrope. Decreasing pressure in the rectiﬁer also decreases
the temperature diﬀerence between the sections, and as such the driving force for the
heat transfer from the rectiﬁer to the stripper. The result should be less total conden-
sation in the rectiﬁer and less total evaporation in the stripper. This might lead to less
separation, but the component ﬂuxes are diﬃcult to predict because they have opposed
the total material ﬂux in earlier results. Increasing the pressure should lead to overall
higher condensation and evaporation in the rectiﬁer and stripper, respectively.
89
==PAGE:90==

Figure 12.9 shows just the ethanol ﬂux to not clutter the plot too much. The base case
with higher values of kV and P R is not unambiguously better in terms of the actual
separation task, but features overall more extreme values of ethanol interface ﬂux, in
many positions further from 0 than the other cases. It is diﬃcult to say which proﬁle is
better, but it is interesting to note that the ethanol ﬂux in the rectiﬁer with decreased P R
is entirely in the negative. As pointed out earlier, this means that the ethanol is moving
from the liquid to the vapour, which is favourable, but not commonly the purpose of a
rectiﬁer.

but experience would say that such a steep proﬁle is unusual at best. It is undoubtedly
an advantage, computations wise, to reduce the stiﬀness of the system. Reducing the
pressure is starting to look advantages for a variety of reasons.

13
Discussion
The simulations obtained are a good step towards modelling a rate-based heat integrated
distillation system, but there are still elements that need to be added in order to fully
describe, design, and control such a unit.
13.1
Geometry
The most encompassing element is the system’s geometry. Model 2 implies no geom-
etry, but the symmetric ﬂows leads to the vertical visualisation as the most apparent
understanding.
The ﬂows and tube wall are also thought to be ﬂat, not curved, an
assumption which would be reasonable at fairly high tube diameter or thin liquid ﬂows.
In a geometry where the liquid ﬂows, or tube wall, are thick compared to the diameter
of the inner tube, the area of the interface in the stripper is considerably lower than
the area of the interface in the rectiﬁer, given the same distance down the tube. Model
2 currently cannot handle such a discrepancy because the independent variable, a, is
assumed equal in size in any equation is is used. It might be possible to correct this
by using a simple correction factor which adjusts the eﬀective area relative to a known
standard area, like the area of the inner tube wall at a given length. The heat integration
could also beneﬁt from such correction, because the liquid temperatures T L,S (a) and
T L,R (a) may not actually be located such that they form the temperature driving force.
In some geometries there will be a signiﬁcant displacement, which can be corrected for
(using correction factors for each aﬀected variable), but at some point it might be better
to deﬁne the geometry and model it rigorously. The situation is illustrated in ﬁgure 13.1.
99
==PAGE:100==
13.1. GEOMETRY
Vapour flow
Liquid flow
Vapour flow
Liquid flow
Tube wall
Stripper
(inner tube)
Rectifier
(outer tube)
TL,S(a)
TL,R(a·β)
TL,R(a)
Figure 13.1: Due to the geometry, the area develops diﬀerently
down the tube. T L,S (a) is not aligned with T L,R (a), a problem
which increases with higher a. β corrects for the displacement.
Such a correction factor has also been suggested as a means of correcting for a diﬀerence
between heat and mass transfer area, in cases where they are not equal [4].
The lack of diameter also means the liquid and vapour velocities cannot be determined.
The velocities are very important for how the interface behaves and how the heat and
mass transfer coeﬃcients should be corrected. It is of particular importance to identify
if the ﬂows are turbulent, and whether or not this is location dependent.
In conventional distillation theory, the concepts of ﬂooding and entrainment are ex-
tremely important.
They cannot be compared directly, because this concept has no
trays, but there still needs to be a balance between the liquid and the vapour ﬂow rates,
such that the vapour does not blow droplets of liquid in the wrong direction and the liq-
uid allows enough space for the vapour to ﬂow through. Like in a conventional column,
this relates directly to the diameter of the column.
Horizontal Tubes
Rethinking the concept in a horizontal geometry likely means the ﬂows cannot be as-
sumed to have the same symmetry. Figure 13.2 shows the clear diﬀerence between the
mass exchange surface and the heat exchange surface. Furthermore, these surfaces also
change with increased or decreased ﬂow rate, because the height of the liquid changes.
The shell of this tube is heated (which means it is the stripper tube), and the heat
transfer could be of diﬀerent magnitude where the bulk liquid is in contact with the hot
surface compared to where it is not. Note that, unlike the system shown in ﬁgure 4.2 on
page 22, if the upper section of the inner tube wall is not wetted, the vapour will be in
100
==PAGE:101==
CHAPTER 13. DISCUSSION
contact with the hot surface. This changes the mathematical coupling needed to model
the heat integration, making it overall more complicated.
Heat exchange
Mass exchange
Liquid
Vapour

Figure 13.2: Horizontal orientation ﬂow geometry (in the in-
ner tube, which is the stripper in this case).
Green chord is
liquid/vapour interface, red arc is bulk liquid in contact with
hot surface, purple arc is vapour in contact with hot surface.
There is also the situation where some sort of agitator is involved, splashing the bulk
liquid to droplets up into the vapour (ﬁgure 13.3). As shown by the green outlines of the
droplets, these add to the interface area. Droplets on the tube wall might also change
the heat exchange characteristics to be more similar to those at the bulk liquid.

13.2
Improving Computations
Section 11.1 explains some issues that have occurred during the MATLAB computations.
As the system increases in size it becomes more and more important to trim the math
and the code wherever possible, such that the simulations can be computed without
too high requirement of the equipment. The current state of the code is so heavy the
requirement for memory exceeds 6 GB of RAM. Even if the memory requirement is met,
it requires a powerful CPU to execute the code in a tolerable time frame. Tasks related
to design and optimisation, are currently impractical, because such tasks often require
101
==PAGE:102==
13.3. OPTIMISATION OPTIONS
solving the model many times with diﬀerent values. Like the optimisation of the feed
position in the conventional column described on page 88. Even minor studies, such
as the parameter study in section 12.3, are severely limited by computation time. To
remedy this, a few key points have been identiﬁed. The number of discretisation steps,
N, is directly related to this issue. Even though the accuracy of the simulation increases
with high N, it is impractical to depend on a very high value to even get convergence and
preliminary results. A high value of N is required for convergence when steep proﬁles
occur, which is evident in the plots depicting T L in the coupled system (ﬁgure 12.4 for
instance). Therefore, it would ease computation requirements if the T L proﬁles were
made less steep. There are a few ways to achieve this.
One could decrease the heat transfer coeﬃcient over the tube wall, h0, as this would
slow down the heat exchange, making the proﬁle less steep. However, a high h0 is de-
sired because heat integration is one of the major points of the concept. It has been
mentioned that the current value is unrealistically high, though. Whatever the case, h0
is not a value that can just be determined by an operator. It is strictly determined by
the material, shape, and thickness of the tube, and the thermal resistance of the liquid
ﬁlms on the tube.
The inlet temperatures could instead be adjusted. It seems the temperature of the liq-
uid coming into the top of the rectiﬁer should be decreased and/or the temperature of
the liquid coming into the top of the stripper should be increased. This can be done
by changing the outlet temperatures of the condenser and the valve, but it may also
be achieve by decreasing the pressure in the rectiﬁer. The last option looks the most
promising.
In the case that ﬁxing the steepness of T L is not enough to satisfactorily decrease the
computation time it may be necessary to look at the issues in a more mathematically
informed view. This would likely require the consultation of actual mathematicians,
and software engineers might be able to identify problems with the algorithms available
in MATLAB and advise on how to move beyond MATLAB or use it more eﬃciently. More
advanced discretisation schemes or methods for automation of adaptive step-size could
be the kind of tools involved in reducing the simulation time and perhaps the accuracy.
13.3
Optimisation Options
After the simulation time has been reduced to something more manageable, it becomes
interesting to look at developing an actual design procedure, preferably one that deter-
mines optimised operating conditions. Such a design procedure should take the separa-
tion task speciﬁcations - e.g. feed ﬂow rate, feed composition, top product purity, and
bottom product purity - as input arguments and systematically determine the operating
conditions required to perform the task. Such problems have a range of solutions and
ﬁnding which solution in the range is best is the optimisation problem. The optimisation
problem can be generalised by deﬁning a cost function, based on an economical model
weighing capital cost (equipment) and operating cost (duties) appropriately, and then
minimising that cost function using the available input parameters listed in table 10.1
(with the exception of F and xF
e which are task speciﬁcations): A, P R, P S, TRR, and,
BRR. Of these inputs, P S will likely be locked to a value that is known to spare the
enzymes and BRR will be set as low as possible to avoid cells from burning onto the
hot surface in the reboiler. The reboiler duty should also be low because the unit should
102
==PAGE:103==
CHAPTER 13. DISCUSSION
rely on the heat integration between the sections. This leaves A, P R, and TRR for the
optimiser to tweak in order to minimise the cost function. P R can likely be optimised
in an absolute sense, but A and TRR represent a typical compromise between capital
and operational cost. Feed location has also been mentioned in regards to optimisation,
but like the conventional design this is no compromise. It should be straight forward (in
theory if not programming) to ﬁnd the optimal feed location, especially seeing as there
are a discrete number of "locations" due to the discretisation of the model.
13.4
Modelling Auxiliary Units
The compressor, valve, and the mixing with the feed have not been modelled in a way
that includes the energy aspects of those processes. This aspect needs to be added to
those equations for several reasons. The most immediate reason is to get rid of the im-
plicit temperature adjustment that follows these processes, as shown in ﬁgures 12.6a and
12.6b, which is an obstacle for proper design and optimisation as those "heat exchangers"
are not part of the actual concept. Another reason is that without modelling the energy
requirements directly, but needing to calculate those in a separate piece of software,
it is virtually impossible to program any optimisation algorithms. However, removing
these temperature adjustments requires tackling the two-phased outlet expected from
the expansion valve. This can be handled by mixing with a cold feed, but that relies on
assumptions regarding the feed properties and that it is cold enough. The compressor
should be less of a problem as it produces vapour with no liquid (compressors often
cannot endure liquids).
13.5
Other Model Elaborations
A few additional, isolated points may be worth investigating in an endeavour to make
the model more rigorous.
In the current state, Model 1 (and thus Model 2) does not take solids, or pseudo-solids
such as cells and proteins, into considerations. It is not unlikely that cells in a fermen-
tation broth could aﬀect the various transfer coeﬃcients because they can increase the
viscosity. Because they are only present in the stripper, two sets of transfer coeﬃcients
might be necessary. On a similar note, the transfer coeﬃcients could be functions of
ﬂuid and ﬂow properties, but predicting transfer coeﬃcients in this way could also prove
to be too bothersome and still not accurate.
The pure component phase-speciﬁc heat capacities are currently being computed at
some manually selected temperatures that are supposed to represent the applied tem-
perature range. This means that constant heat capacities for ethanol in liquid, ethanol
in vapour, water in liquid, and water in vapour are determined once and for all, and
not updated to match actual temperature. It would be more thorough to incorporate
the temperature dependence at every point in the model where they are used. Heat
capacities of the mixture of ethanol and water are also being simpliﬁed to the mole frac-
tion based weighted average of the pure compound heat capacities, which for the highly
associating pair of ethanol and water is probably a rough estimate. Another issue with
the associating nature of the pair is the calculation of the molar density of the liquid,
cL
t , used in equation 5.3 on page 28. This is currently a constant, simpliﬁed to the molar
103
==PAGE:104==
13.5. OTHER MODEL ELABORATIONS
density of pure water. This can be made slightly more accurate by using a weighted av-
erage between water and ethanol, but to be really exact, some model for excess volume
(or other applicable excess property) needs to be applied as well.
Heats of evaporation are also currently constants.
These might be more accurately
modelled with the Clapeyron equation. These changes should be introduced in a fashion
where the model derivation itself takes them into account, if deemed necessary. Equa-
tions such as the vapour energy ﬂow equation for Model 1 (equation 5.16 on page 31)
is derived with the assumption of constant heat capacity [11] and from a strict mathe-
matical view, this needs to be incorporated by going back to the original derivation and
carry it out without that assumption. Doing that however, requires strong incentive to
further the realism of that speciﬁc point of the model.
104
==PAGE:105==
14
Conclusion
A novel rate-based heat integrated distillation system conﬁguration has been deﬁned, a
model has been derived describing the conﬁguration, and the model has been solved us-
ing numerical methods to obtain simulation results. To solve the model, MATLAB scripts
have been developed from scratch.
This novel conceptual unit operation is inspired by proposed units from patents that
date back many years, but have yet to be modelled. This type of unit might play a
role in separations that involve ﬂuids with solids or liquids too viscous for conventional
columns. Second generation bio-ethanol has been a focus and a relevant case for two
primary reasons: Fermentation broths usually require centrifugation to remove the cells
before further process and the pressure diﬀerence inherent in this concept could be used
to spare the enzymes, used in production of second generation bio-ethanol, from high
temperatures that cause thermal denaturing.
The conﬁguration depicts a stripping section inside a rectifying section, sharing a tube
wall heat integrating the two sections along their entire length. The rectiﬁer operates
at elevated pressure compared to the stripper making the thermal driving force from
rectiﬁer to stripper. A compressor and a valve connect the streams between the top
of the stripper and the bottom of the rectiﬁer. The conﬁguration may be considered
vertical due to symmetrical ﬂows.
Model 1 was derived to model a single section, either stripper or rectiﬁer depending
on speciﬁcations. The model employs the assumption of an interface between the liquid
and vapour phases, at which thermodynamic equilibrium is assumed. Material ﬂuxes
across the interface form the basis for the separation process. Model 1 uses a simpliﬁed
coolant or heating ﬂuid to represent what is in the other section. The liquid and vapour
ﬂows in both sections are counter current spawning the issue of inhomogeneous boundary
conditions. This was circumvented using a discretisation scheme, turning all equations
into algebraic equations, that are solved simultaneously, unlike step wise construction of
solutions common in ODE solvers.
Model 2 (the ﬁnal model) is a coupling of two sets of Model 1, one for each section, with
some additional speciﬁcations relating to reﬂux, reboil, mechanical vapour recompres-
sion, and the pressure reduction valve. The speciﬁcations are on the form of additional
algebraic equations, so the same general solution method applies. The coolant or heat-
ing ﬂuid in Model 1 is removed, as both sections are now modelled. This allows the use
of temperatures in the diﬀerent sections to form the temperature gradient in the heat
transfer equations, thus modelling the heat integration.
Simulations have been successful and the results are qualitatively sensible. Variations
to certain parameters also resulted in reasonable eﬀects.
A speciﬁc separation task was simulated, separating 100 mol/s feed with 3 mol% ethanol
105
==PAGE:106==
into an ethanol product stream containing 83.4 mol% ethanol, limited by the azeotropic
composition modelled to be at ∼86 mol% ethanol. The ethanol production rate was
3.2 mol/s. The water product was 99.7 mol% water at a rate of 96.8 mol/s. The mass
balance closes fairly well, considering the numerical nature of the solving method. This
separation was achieved with very high reﬂux ratio at the top of the rectiﬁer; the reﬂux
stream is thirty times larger than the product stream. The reboil ratio was also higher
than desired, considering one of the key aspects of the concept unit is removing/reduc-
ing condenser and reboiler duties via heat integration between the sections. The ratio
between production rate at the bottom and the amount of reboil was 1. Considering the
high bottom production rate, that is a lot of reboiler duty. The required area was 300
m2 of rectiﬁer and 300 m2 of stripper. With smooth tubes and no rotor that is a very
large piece of equipment for a very small task. The eﬀective area needs to be increased,
but there are ways to do that such as the mentioned rotor, non-smooth tube surfaces, or
packings in the rectifying section. The pressures in the stripper and the rectiﬁer were set
to 1 atm. and 2.5 bar, respectively, to ensure the temperature gradient facilitated heat
integration no matter the composition in the integrated liquid ﬂows. There are indica-
tions that this pressure diﬀerence is needlessly high and the advantages of operating the
stripper at sub-atmospheric pressure have been mentioned. It is positive that δP ≈1.5
bar is too high. This means it might be possible to achieve satisfactory separation with
the rectiﬁer at atmospheric pressure, which is cheap, and the stripper at 0.2 bar, which
should spare the enzymes from thermal denaturing.
Quick comparison to a conventional distillation column showed that the novel concept
requires double the energy inputs (and removal). However, the operating and design
conditions of area, pressures, reﬂux and reboil ratios are not chosen systematically to
meet the separation task speciﬁcations. This requires a proper design procedure which
is yet to be deﬁned. On a similar note, no optimisation has been performed. With a
more systematic method resulting in an optimised operation the energy aspect of the
novel concept will be much more favourable.
Potential optimisations of the novel concept include adjustments of area, reﬂux, reboil,
pressures, and feed location. Optimisation needs to consider capital and operational
costs to arrive at a compromise between the total tube area and the reﬂux and reboil
ratios. Stripper pressure is likely determined by the consideration for the enzymes, and
the rectiﬁer pressure may have an absolute optimal point which is not a compromise.
The feed location also does not change capital cost and can be optimised for low op-
erating cost. Tube area, and tube geometry, are also vital design parameters when it
comes to energy demand. It is a future goal to be able to model more complex interiors
in order to employ a rotor and other area enhancing features such as column packing
materials, available for use in the rectiﬁer which processes no solids.
The size of the mathematical system has led to very high computation demands, com-
pletely surpassing the capabilities of low-end computers. Mid- to high-end computers
solve the system with some speciﬁcations, but seem to stall in situations that should
be solvable. This issue has limited the analysis of the model, but the major obstacles
have been deﬁned and options for removing those obstacles are suggested. Reducing the
pressure in the rectiﬁer, which seems to be a good idea overall, should be the ﬁrst step
in reducing computation requirements.

==PAGE:107==

111
==PAGE:112==
A.2. MODIFICATIONS
A.2
Modiﬁcations
A synthetic change that the reader should be aware of is that the component subscript
1 will be change to e for ethanol, and 2 will be changed to w for water. This will limit
confusion when subscripts for positions along the discrete spatial axis are introduced
later. The area variable A will be changed to a, and A is now the largest value of a. So
the variable a goes from 0 to A.
Equations F10 and F13, the diﬀerential equations for yV
2 and yV
1 are removed and re-
placed by sum equations, as this seemed to make convergence easier. Both methods
should work, in theory, but because of the nature of the numerical solution method, the
strict adherence to the sum of the mole fractions is preferable.
A more substantial change will also be made: all the auxiliary functions (not phase equi-
libria related) equations will be included in the equations. The reason for this change
is to avoid scattering the model across several pages. This will also make discretisation
easier as all the variables in each equation are immediately visible. This may make the
model even more intimidating, but I ﬁnd it is much easier to actually apply this way.
Another major change is the implementation of the T L variable, which is the tempera-
ture of the liquid. This was previously omitted because it was not strictly needed for the
description of the process. For the heat integrated process, however, it will be needed
to determine the driving temperature across the tube wall. T I is not good enough for
that purpose, for a few reasons. The ﬁrst reason is the heat transfer coeﬃcient should
also take into account the liquid layers. This is doable, but not ideal. The second, more
crucial reason is the interface has no volume and thus not heat capacity. Without heat
capacity, the eﬀect on the temperature - due to heat transfer - cannot be described. All
in all, it was probably a mistake to omit T L in the ﬁrst place, as it serves many purposes.
The order of the equations will also be changed as will the naming of the equations.
In the modiﬁed version, F equations are algebraic equations. G equations will be dif-
ferential equations in variables that ﬂow in the positive a direction, H equations are for
variables that ﬂow in the negative a direction.

Steps 2 and 3

and T C,S. Step 3 changes any dependence on T C to a dependence on T L in the opposite
section. For instance, T C,R becomes T L,S.

B.3. STEP 4
B.3
Step 4
Step 4 connects the stream using a compressor, a valve, and deﬁned reﬂux ratios. A
proper feed is also added to the system. The equations established in step 3 are left
unchanged, but more are added to complete the model, by connecting those ﬂows.
B.4
Compressor
The vapour from the top of the stripper is compressed before it is let into the bottom
of the rectiﬁer. This compression simpliﬁed to the following set of equations, where
energy is neglected in terms of proper modelling. The outlet temperature is calculated
to the dew point temperature at those conditions, which can be achieved with cooling
or heating, even if it is not the wisest thing to do. This is a point that could be handled
more rigorously, but this simpliﬁcation made convergence easier.
C1 ≡yV,R
e
(A) −yV,S
e
(0) = 0
(B.1)
C2 ≡V R (A) −V S (0) = 0
(B.2)
C3 ≡T V,R (A) −Td

yV,S
e
,Ae,Aw,P R
= 0
(B.3)
Equation B.3 is more a procedure of MATLAB functions than a mathematical equation. Td
is a function for the dew point temperature, which is dependent on the composition, the
pressure, and the thermodynamic model. At any rate, it serves the purpose of setting the
output from the compressor to the dew point temperature. This is somewhat arbitrary,
but it settles the math.
B.5
Valve
The valve is handled similarly to the compressor, although the feed stream is handled
in the same equations, even though that would practically take place in a separate
mixing unit. The ﬂow rate of the feed is assumed constant (F) as is the ethanol content
(xF
e ). The temperature from the valve is set to the bubble point temperature, again for
simplicity and because the tools are already available in the developed code.
V1 ≡xL,S
e
(0) −xF
e F + xL,R
e
(A) LR (A)
LS (0)
= 0
(B.4)
V2 ≡LR + F −LS = 0
(B.5)
V3 ≡T L,S −Tb

xL,S
e
(0) , Ae, Aw, P S
= 0
(B.6)
Like equation B.3, equation B.6 is a procedure which determines the bubble point tem-
perature of the liquid coming from the valve and mixed with the feed. An isenthalpic
pressure reduction would likely outlet a stream in the two-phase region, which is not
handy for the model. The mixing with the feed, however, actually makes this tempera-
ture somewhat realistic, as the feed can be pre-heated by the hot valve stream, bringing
the mixture to the bubble point temperature.
122
==PAGE:123==
APPENDIX B. FROM MODEL 1 TO MODEL 2
B.6
Top Reﬂux
The vapour outlet from the top of the rectiﬁer is cooled (not rigorously modelled) and
split into a product stream and a reﬂux stream. The amount of material reﬂuxed is
the product ﬂow rate times the reﬂux ratio. The product ﬂow rate is not an existing
variable, so this relationship is manipulated a little to get equation B.13. The ethanol
contents are of course equal. The temperature is set to the bubble point as with the
valve. TRR is the top reﬂux ratio.
Reflux1 ≡LR (0) + LR (0)
TRR −V R (0) = 0
(B.7)

= 0
(B.9)
B.7
Bottom Reboil
Some of the bottoms product is likewise reboiled, sending a fraction back and the re-
maining out as product. The same principles apply. The temperature is the dew point,
BRR is the bottom reboil ratio.

Operating Conditions
Model 2 is simpliﬁed in one aspect compared to using two times Model 1: Inputs. Model
2 needs fewer boundary conditions speciﬁed, because a lot more is now connected. The
conditions that can be tweaked more closely resemble reality. These are total area, the
two pressures, the reﬂux, and reboil ratio. Those are the tools available for separating
a feed ﬂow with a given ethanol content.

Matlab Code for Thermodynamics
The code is also handed in electronically, sorted in folder for each task.
All folders
contain a main script. Users should run this script in MATLAB to get started. Plotting
is primarily done via gnuplot. The gnuplot scripts are also included in the hand-in, but
they need modiﬁcation before they will run due to ﬁle paths and references.

function f = MargulesFit(x,y,T,A,P,Q)
2
3
e = x;
4
y1 = y;
5
x1 = x;
6
x2 = 1−x;
7
K = [x x];
8
9
for i = 1:size(x,1)
10
K(i,:) = Kvalue([x1(i) x2(i)], A(1), A(2), T(i), P);
11

12

13
14
e(:,1) = (y1−y);
15
e=Q*e;
16
f = sum(sum(e.^2));

K-value Function
1
function K = Kvalue(x, A12, A21, T, P)
2
3
%Calculating gamma values using the Margules equation
4
Q1 = A12*x(2)^2 + 2*A21*x(1)*x(2);
5
Q2 = A21*x(1)^2 + 2*A12*x(2)*x(1);
6
7
Q = 1/3 * (x(1)*Q1 + x(2)*Q2);
8
9
gamma = [0 ; 0];
10
gamma(1) = exp(Q1 −2*Q);
11
gamma(2) = exp(Q2 −2*Q);
12
129
==PAGE:130==
13
%gamma = [1 ; 1];
14
15
%Antoine equation used to determine Psat
16
Psat = [0 ; 0];
17
18
% Psat(1) = exp(23.402 −3593.4/(T−34.29)); % methanol
19
Psat(1) = exp(22.85387084 −3298.513013/(T−61.819)); % ethanol (1st set ...
of parameters)
20
Psat(1) = exp(23.59405985 −3681.080618/(T−46.424)); % ethanol (2nd set ...
of parameters)
21
Psat(1) = exp(23.88308033 −3846.258863/(T−40.191)); % ethanol (3rd set ...
of parameters)
22
Psat(2) = exp(23.196 −3816.4/(T−46.13)); % water
23
24
%Finally, Kvalues are calculated
25
K = [0 ; 0];
26
27
K(1) = gamma(1)*Psat(1)/P;
28
K(2) = gamma(2)*Psat(2)/P;
29
30
end

12

13
14
end

3

10
11
end

function F = discretisation(x,BCs,p,c,in,N)

%% Extract Variables from X vector and BCs

==PAGE:142==

[xIe, xIw, yIe, yIw, TI, Ne, Nw, L, xLe, xLw, TL, TC, V, yVe, yVw, TV] = ...

xLe(i), xLw(i), p, h);

TL(i), p,
c,

h);

Ne(i), Nw(i), h);

792 <−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−>
793
function K = Kvalue(x, A12, A21, T, P)
794
795
%Calculating gamma values using the Margules equation
796
Q1 = A12*x(2)^2 + 2*A21*x(1)*x(2);
797
Q2 = A21*x(1)^2 + 2*A12*x(2)*x(1);
798
799
Q = 1/3 * (x(1)*Q1 + x(2)*Q2);
800
801
gamma = [0 ; 0];
802
gamma(1) = exp(Q1 −2*Q);
803
gamma(2) = exp(Q2 −2*Q);
804
805
%gamma = [1 ; 1];
806

1080
function K = Kvalue(x, A12, A21, T, P)
1081
1082
%Calculating gamma values using the Margules equation
1083
Q1 = A12*x(2)^2 + 2*A21*x(1)*x(2);
1084
Q2 = A21*x(1)^2 + 2*A12*x(2)*x(1);
1085
1086
Q = 1/3 * (x(1)*Q1 + x(2)*Q2);
1087
1088
gamma = [0 ; 0];
1089
gamma(1) = exp(Q1 −2*Q);
1090
gamma(2) = exp(Q2 −2*Q);
1091
1092
%gamma = [1 ; 1];
1093
1094
%Antoine equation used to determine Psat
1095
Psat = [0 ; 0];
1096
1097
% Psat(1) = exp(23.402 −3593.4/(T−34.29)); % methanol
1098
Psat(1) = exp(22.85387084 −3298.513013/(T−61.819)); % ethanol (1st set ...
of parameters)
1099
Psat(1) = exp(23.59405985 −3681.080618/(T−46.424)); % ethanol (2nd set ...
of parameters)
1100
Psat(1) = exp(23.88308033 −3846.258863/(T−40.191)); % ethanol (3rd set ...
of parameters)
1101
Psat(2) = exp(23.196 −3816.4/(T−46.13)); % water
1102
1103
%Finally, Kvalues are calculated
1104
K = [0 ; 0];
1105
1106
K(1) = gamma(1)*Psat(1)/P;
1107
K(2) = gamma(2)*Psat(2)/P;
1108
1109
end

Entire Report of Pre-Study

==PAGE:260==
Simulation of Rate-Based Separation Processes
Modelling Two-Component Condenser
Morten Jesper Greisen Larsen - s090041
23rd of January 2015
==PAGE:261==
Abstract
Condensation of a binary vapour mixture of methanol and water in a cooled tube has
been modelled and simulated. Starting with reproduction of a textbook example, the
model was made increasingly complex in order to describe the proﬁle down the tube and
to model the dynamics.
The textbook example was reproduced with a focus on compiling the necessary the-
ory in a short and cohesive fashion after which the modelling of the radial material and
energy transport was made almost trivial. The results were reproduced perfectly and a
strong foundation for further development was established. The model itself was, at this
point, a system of seven algebraic equations solving the liquid-vapour interface proper-
ties.
The model was expanded to model the behaviour down the area of the tube, making the
model a system of eight diﬀerential and the same (almost) seven algebraic equations,
solving the same variables as above as well as axial ﬂows and bulk phase properties. Dif-
ferent ﬂow patterns are discussed and simulations for the co-current system are provided,
showing the model produces realistic results. When agitated with changes in parame-
ters, the model reacted appropriately indicating correct dependency on said parameters.
MATLAB ﬁles make up a part of this project and are available for simulation of co-current
as well as counter-current systems.
A dynamic model has been developed by adding accumulated terms to the existing
diﬀerential equations thereby making all eight equations partial diﬀerential equations,
where one might simplify to an ordinary diﬀerential equations. This system was not
simulated as no solution has been developed, however, the equations were tested to see
if they reduced correctly to their steady state equivalents, which they did. Solution was
discussed, but it does seem to be a task of some diﬃculty.
Assumptions made during the development were evaluated and the theoretical foun-
dation was questioned in a brief discussion section, providing some basis for improving
the models in order to increase credibility or make reasonable simpliﬁcations.
2

==PAGE:264==
1
Introduction
The purpose of this report is to develop mathematical models of increasing complexity
in order to simulate condensation of a binary mixture vapour, consisting of methanol
and water, on the cooled wall of a tube. The application of such a condensation process
could be rate based separation of the ﬂuids utilising that the two components do not
condense with the same rate.
The ﬁrst model solves the one-dimensional mass transport of the vapour components
to a liquid condensate on the tube wall and the energy transport from the vapour,
through the condensate and the wall to the cooling media surrounding the tube. This
provides the solution at a position in the tube where certain variables are known, such
as at the inlet. Not only is this model part of the more complex models, it also provides
the initial guess used to solve the following models numerically. This model consists
solely of algebraic equations.
Following that is the development of a model which enables simulation of the system
down the tube. This introduces the spatial variable A which is the inner surface area of
the tube. Area is chosen instead of length down the tube, because this allows the ge-
ometry of the tube to be ignored, at least for now. At this point, the system is assumed
in steady state, which means the system consists of ordinary diﬀerential equations in
combination with the previously established algebraic equations. The total system is a
system of diﬀerential-algebraic-equations, and is possible to solve using Matlab.
Finally, the system is expanded to include time-dependency. This is done by setting
up control volumes and adding terms of accumulation to the diﬀerent mass and energy
balances.
This changes some of the equations’ type to partial diﬀerential equations,
which drastically increases the diﬃculty of solving the system. The system is not solved
in this report, but the subject is explored in preparation for future work.
5
==PAGE:265==
2
System Description
The systems modelled in this report are all variations of a condensing vapour ﬂowing
through a cooled tube. The ﬁrst system, which is adapted from "Example 15.1.1 Con-
densation of a Methanol-Water Mixture" pages 442-448 in [1], is a simple start where
the condensate is stagnant because it has yet to accumulate enough material to start
ﬂowing. The radial mass and energy transport phenomena are of interest here, that is
the transport perpendicular to the tube length. This system is only realistic as long as
the amount of condensate is small and is modelled and solved mostly as a preparatory
exercise in order to have some foundation for the more comprehensive model. Figure 2.1
Vapour (V)
Coolant (C)
Condensate film (L)
Condensate film (L)
Tube wall (W)
Coolant (C)
Tube wall (W)
Axial direction
Radial direction
Figure 2.1: Cooled tube with vapour ﬂow, tube wall, conden-
sate layer, and coolant ﬂow. Directions terminology indicated.
presents this initial system without any ﬂow because ﬂows are irrelevant at this point -
it is a solution at a given point.
The system is expanded with vapour, condensate, and coolant ﬂows, in ﬁgure 2.2,
to consider what happens down the tube, not just at a point. Like before, the mass and
energy transfer perpendicular to the ﬂow is solved, and equations are needed to calculate
how the ﬂows change through the tube. The co-current direction of all the ﬂows makes
this system simple with regards to boundary conditions as inlet conditions are usually
available and they are all deﬁned at the same end.
Figure 2.3 shows a potentially more realistic ﬂow pattern involving a counter-current
ﬂow because, in some cases, this provides better mass and energy exchange. It is usually
a lot more useful to deﬁne the inlets of all the ﬂows, but this is made very diﬃcult when
the vapour inlet is not at A = 0, but at the end of the tube. This makes the counter-
current system a lot more diﬃcult to apply in a useful fashion, because the easiest thing
to do is to deﬁne the counter-ﬂowing stream, in this case the vapour, at A = 0. The
model itself is a little bit diﬀerent with counter-current ﬂow and it may be necessary to
consider diﬀerent transport phenomena between phases that ﬂow in opposite directions.
These physical diﬀerences add to the mathematical diﬃculty of the boundary condition
6

of detail.

umes relevant for the time dependant equation system.
In reality, the radius of the
vapour cylinder may be diﬃcult to obtain and is probably not a constant value. For the
sake of simpliﬁcation, it can be set arbitrarily to some constant value.

==PAGE:268==
3
Mass and Energy Transport
The developed models rely heavily on mass and energy transport theory. This chapter
describes the origin of the diﬀerent elements of the models one by one and explains how
they are combined. There is also a short note on calculation of the thermodynamic
equilibrium.
yI
Liquid film
Bulk vapour
Vapour film
xL
xI
yV
TV
TI
TL
N

Figure 3.1: Mass and energy ﬂuxes across a two-dimensional
interface. The amount of condensate may amount to a diﬀerence
between the bulk liquid and liquid at the interface.
Consider the system on ﬁgure 3.1 adapted from [1]. It illustrates the assumption that
there exists an interfacial plane on which the components are in thermodynamic equilib-
rium. The assumption of equilibrium is justiﬁed by the interface’s lack of volume, which
means the system instantly equilibrates at the interface. The interfacial mole fractions
are then calculated using a suiting thermodynamic model. The interfacial mole fractions
are important because they contribute to the mass transfer driving forces.
3.1
Mass transfer
Another consequence of the interface assumption is that the ﬂux is in continuity across
the system. Since the interface has no volume it cannot accumulate matter and so the
ﬂux into the interface is the same as the ﬂux out of it. The ﬂux through the diﬀerent
sections then equal each other i.e. NV = NI = NL = N and there is no need to use more
than one symbol for the total ﬂux N. Flux continuity also applies to the component
ﬂuxes. Assuming the tube wall is inert, the material entering the liquid phase does not
leave.
9
==PAGE:269==
3.2. ENERGY TRANSFER
The component ﬂuxes are made up of a diﬀusive term driven by a diﬀerence in compo-
sition and a convective term driven by precipitation caused by energy transfer. On page
280 of [1], the ﬂuxes are described by the following equations, where J is the diﬀusive
ﬂux and N is the total convective ﬂux:

3.2
Energy Transfer
The arguments made for material ﬂux (N) continuity also apply to the energy ﬂux,
E. The energy transfers further because it conducts through the wall so the following
is assumed true. It is true at steady state operation, and may be assumed true in a
pseudo-steady state conception of the system where the dynamics for the radial transfer
are fast compared to the axial transfer.
EC = EW = EL = EI = EV
10
==PAGE:270==
CHAPTER 3. MASS AND ENERGY TRANSPORT
Two mechanisms contribute to the transfer of energy. The ﬁrst is heat conduction, q,
where energy moves from a warm material to a colder one. The other mechanism is
the displacement of energy with material that changes from one phase to another. This
displacement is the sum of the enthalpy (function of temperature), H (T), moved by the
material ﬂux, N:

the Margules model for binary systems to calculate the activity coeﬃcients, γ1 and γ2,
and using those to calculate the K values. The K values also require the saturation
pressures, P sat, calculated via the Antoine equation. Without going into too much de-
tail, the applied method is presented below.

4.7. SOLVING THE SYSTEM
Importance of Initial Guess
In table 4.7 below is a ludicrous guess which was still solvable, showing that the system
does converge toward the correct solution even when strained. The temperature guess,
however, cannot get too low, or the system becomes very diﬃcult to solve. With rea-
sonable guesses for the other variables and the temperature set to 20◦C, the system fails
to converge toward a solution. A mole fraction guess of 2 is also problematic, but that
is a rather terrible guess anyway. It seems any mole fraction guess between 0 and 1 is

5
Expanding the Model in Axial Direction
Moving on to the models required to describe the systems presented in ﬁgures 2.2 and
2.3, we look to pages 462-464 of Taylor&Krishna [1]. The systems are still assumed to be
in steady state operation, a useful assumption when simulating or designing continuous
operation and not being responsible for controlling the unit.
The seven algebraic equations in 4.8 are used without alterations, except for one; F3.
This is reverted to its more general version, equation 3.2, to move away from the as-
sumption that the liquid composition is the same as the interface composition. This also
introduces the extra variables xL
1 and xL
2 .
In addition to the system obtained thus far, equations are needed to describe how the
variables in table 4.2 change down the tube. The mole fractions must depend on the
amount of vapour ﬂowing, so the molar vapour ﬂow, V , needs to be introduced as well.
Similarly, molar liquid ﬂow, L, must be introduced because the liquid ﬁlm will increase,
and probably start to ﬂow, when V condenses. For the sake of simplicity, the pressure
will not be modelled but rather set to a constant, 1 atm.
This means that a total of eight new variables are introduced adding to the existing
seven. The complete set of variables is listed in table 5.1. The spatial variable used to
model the system down the tube is area, A, which circumvents the need for clarifying
the geometry.
Table 5.1: Solved variables

L
xL
1
xL
2
Flow Direction
This report deals with two diﬀerent ﬂow patterns illustrated in ﬁgure 2.2, co-current
ﬂow, and ﬁgure 2.3, counter-current ﬂow. In the co-current ﬂow pattern, the vapour
phase loses material to the liquid phase as the area increases. In counter-current ﬂow,
the vapour phase gains material from the liquid phase as the area increases. This does
not mean that the ﬂux is negative, but that the vapour loses material in its ﬂow direction
which is opposite that of the area increase. The result of this is only a few diﬀerences
in sign, so be mindful of this.
Another likely consequence of diﬀerent ﬂow patterns is that the radial transport phe-
nomena, modelled in equation set 4.8, may change signiﬁcantly. This concern is outside
the scope of this report, but may be worth investigating.
19
==PAGE:279==

as described in equations 3.11. Like with F8, F9, and F10, there is a diﬀerent sign for
the counter-current system. Simply change the minus on the right hand side to a plus.
Again, this simple change may not reﬂect the complete implications of the changes in
transport phenomena in the radial direction caused by the two contacting ﬂuids ﬂowing
in diﬀerent directions.

5.5
Solving the System
The system is solved with MATLAB’s ode15s command which is able to take a number
of algebraic equations and a number of diﬀerential equations as arguments via the mass
matrix option. A few key results are presented in the ﬁgures below while the complete
results can be found in appendix A. The coding details can be found in appendix C.3.
The script also has a simple option for solving the counter-current system, which makes
necessary changes to the diﬀerential equations and loads an alternative inlet ﬁle (which
actually speciﬁes the vapour outlet). This option does not change the interface transport
equations.
0
50
100
150
200
250
300
350
400
0
50

150
200
Molar flow [mol/s]
Area [m2]
 
 
Methanol vapour flow
Water vapour flow
Methanol condensate flow
Water condensate flow
Figure 5.1: Molar ﬂow rates in the diﬀerent phases as they
change down the tube.
Figure 5.1 shows that the condenser does in fact condense the vapour. Both components
condense on the cool tube wall, but their rates of condensation are diﬀerent leading to
the basis for a separation process. The separation is more obvious in ﬁgure 5.2 where we
can see the methanol content in the vapour is increased from 40% to some 70%, which
is in agreement with methanol being more volatile than water. It is, however, worth
noting that at A = 400 m2, the vapour has almost completely condensed.
The model produces believable results in the sense that the general trends are as ex-
pected. In ﬁgures 5.3 a few parameter values are changed in order to achieve two things.
The ﬁrst is to test if the model reacts in the way it is supposed to, the other is a pre-
liminary test of the impact of those parameters. This will provide a basis for deciding
where the model needs more detail and which parameters might require experiments to
verify.
The ﬁrst changed parameter is the heat coeﬃcient over the condensate + wall + coolant,
h0, which is likely a very important parameter. It is modelled as a constant, but is prob-
ably changing with the changing thickness of the condensate layer. It is changed from
2000 to 1000 W/m2/K, which means that less heat transfers from the vapour to the
25
==PAGE:285==
5.5. SOLVING THE SYSTEM
0
50
100
150
200
250
300
350
400
0
0.2
0.4
0.6
0.8
1
Mole fractions
Area [m2]
 
 
Methanol in vapour
Water in vapour
Methanol in condensate
Water in condensate
Figure 5.2: Mole fractions changing down the tube.
coolant. The result of this should be that less vapour condensates over a given area.
The second changed parameter is the liquid phase mass transfer coeﬃcient kL, which is
decreased by a factor of 1000 from 0.08 to 0.00008 m/s. This parameter inﬂuences the
diﬀusive ﬂux through the liquid layer thus adding to the component ﬂuxes. Lowering
the value should decrease the diﬀusive ﬂuxes and thus the component ﬂuxes. Thirdly,
the coolant temperature, T C, is lowered from 39.1◦C to 20◦C. This should cause the
condensation to be noticeably faster and require less tube area.
Figure 5.3a shows the mole fraction of methanol in the vapour phase, a measure of
the separation of methanol and water, with the diﬀerent parameter variations. Figure
5.3b shows the total vapour ﬂow with the same variations. As expected, lowering h0
caused less vapour to condense by a signiﬁcant amount and thus a lower degree of sepa-
ration. The rate of condensation is very close to halved by halving h0 requiring almost
200 m2 to condense 150 mol compared to 100 m2 required with the standard parameter
values. The change of kL seems to have only little eﬀect on the overall process. Increas-
ing kL provided the same results as the original value (not plotted). At suﬃciently high
kL it seems there is no diﬀerence between xI and xL, making the value meaningless. At
low values, there is a higher diﬀerence between xI and xL, but just not enough to really
change anything. Expanding the liquid ﬂux equation (5.1) to the general model appears
to have no signiﬁcant meaning. The third parameter change was the coolant temper-
ature, which appears to have a similarly powerful eﬀect compared to h0. This is not
surprising, but it demonstrates that the model reacts to this change as expected. The
methanol vapour mole fraction go towards negative inﬁnity because the molar vapour
ﬂow becomes zero. This is not a major problem; the model is not expected to correctly
calculate a mole fraction that does not exist.

6
Expanding the Model in Time
The following additions to the model are original derivations because Taylor&Krishna
do not deal with adding the dynamics. They are quite important for some applications
such as start-up/shut-down, batch operation, and designing control paradigms.
rti
rto
rC
Coolant
rV

Tube wall

Figure 6.1: Layered presentation of the cylinder shells making
up the geometry of the system. ∆z indicates small axial distance
over which control volumes are deﬁned. rV is the vapour cylinder
radius, rti is the inner tube radius, rto is the outer tube radius,
and rC is the radius from the tube center to the outer edge of
the coolant.
Figure 6.1 recaps the system in a diﬀerent view to get a better understanding of the
control volumes used in the following accumulation terms of the mass and energy bal-
ances. Basically, there is one solid cylinder and three layers of cylinder shells around it.
For this initial take on the dynamic model, all the radii are considered constant.

6.2
Energy Balances
The energy balances use the same control volumes and very similar logic.
Control Volume: Vapour Cylinder
For the vapour ﬂow, the energy accumulation term is in the form of energy density given
by the product of the molar density, cV
t , and the molar enthalpy, HV . Axial energy
ﬂow is molar ﬂow rate, V , multiplied by the molar enthalpy, HV . Energy is lost to the
condensate in the form of energy conduction, qV , and energy convection, i.e. energy
carried with material, given by the sum of the component molar enthalpies, HV
i , and
the component ﬂuxes, Ni.

==PAGE:297==
7
Solving the Dynamic System
The equation system in section 6.3 is a complex system of non-linear algebraic and
non-linear partial diﬀerential equations.
Due to time limitations and a lack of prior
experience with partial diﬀerential equation systems, a solution of this system was not
made. Solving the system requires a proper analysis of the system in order to determine
what kind of solution might be possible. This chapter opens up the discussion of how
this system might be handled.
7.1
Analytical methods to solve partial diﬀerential equa-
tions
Wikipedia summarises a number of methods for solving partial diﬀerential equations an-
alytically, which, if possible, is preferable due to the accuracy of the solution. The section
on methods for non-linear equations, discouragingly, proclaims no generally applicable
methods exist, but that some methods work for some systems.
• Separation of variables (not applicable, requires linear system)
• Method of characteristics
• Integral transform
• Change of variables
• Fundamental solution
• Superposition principle
• Methods for non-linear equations
• Lie group method
• Semi-analytical methods
What would be preferable for the system at hand, is if it were possible to reduce the
PDEs to ODEs and then use the familiar ode15s. A complete analytical solution of the
large system can only be a massively comprehensive task bound for failure. Some of
the methods are extremely speciﬁc while some are more generally applicable. Method
of characteristics is perhaps a good candidate for the co-current system because it is
known to work with ﬂow systems where the inlet is known at all times and the solution
at t = 0 is also known. It is not an easy method and keeping track of the mathematics
across eight PDEs seems daunting.
7.2
Numerical methods to solve partial diﬀerential equa-
tions
Numerical solutions that lose only very little in terms of accuracy, but gain a lot in terms
of speed might be advantageous compared to tedious analytical methods.
38
==PAGE:298==
CHAPTER 7. SOLVING THE DYNAMIC SYSTEM
7.2.1
Discretisation
Numerical solutions may be carried out by approximating discrete bits of the solution on
a suﬃciently small step. This may remove one or more of the variables thus converting
the system to a system of ODEs or even algebraic equations. If done correctly, such
solutions may be very accurate. A variety of approximation schemes exist, useful in
diﬀerent situations such as with diﬀerent types of boundary and initial conditions.
A thing to consider with numerical solutions is that some discretisation schemes require
the equations to be on a speciﬁc form otherwise the solutions will simply be non-physical.
This is in spite of being mathematically equivalent to a form that would produce correct
results with the same scheme. For this reason, an alternative constellation of the system
found in section 6.3 can be found in appendix B. This system is on divergent form, which
should lend directly to a speciﬁc discretisation scheme.[2]
Another hazard of numerical solutions is numerical diﬀusion, a strange phenomenon
causing instability when discretisation steps in diﬀerent discretised variables are not
properly sized compared to each other.
Below are two diﬀerent discretisation schemes; one approximates the diﬀerential quo-
tients of one parameter, in this case A.
∂f
∂A ≈fi+1 −fi−1
2∆A
(7.1)
This scheme is not really suﬃcient, because knowledge of the end-points are required,
at least approximatively. The reason is that each approximation is dependant on the
previous and on the next. Discretising in e.g. 10 steps introduces 12 variables, requiring
two of those to be known. One is known, because the inlet conditions are deﬁned, but
that is not enough.
The other alternative discretises in two variables.
∂f
∂t ≈fk+1
i
−fk
i
∆t
(7.2)
∂f
∂A ≈fk
i −fk
i−1
∆A
(7.3)
Where k is the time step and i is the step in area.
This may actually be a working method, at least for co-current ﬂow, because the solution
can be built up from one end. Counter-current ﬂow likely requires a diﬀerent approach.
7.2.2
MATLAB function
A MATLAB function exists for solving partial diﬀerential equations (pdepe), but it does
not appear to easily handle algebraic equations, even though pdepe does utilize ode15s.
From the MATLAB manual it seems pdepe performs a discretisation which does result
in algebraic terms which are then conveyed automatically to ode15s via the mass ma-
trix option. The option is eﬀectively reserved and unavailable for additional algebraic
equations.
39
==PAGE:299==
7.3. LINEARISATION
7.3
Linearisation
Linearisation of the system is an option suitable for tracking the dynamics only around
a steady state operation, which may be good enough for use in control. This approach
depends on how far from steady state one expects the system to go, due to disturbances
and such, compared to how far from steady state the linear model remains accurate. This
might be hard to determine if the non-linear system cannot be solved for comparison.
A skilled control engineer might be able to handle some model-system discrepancy.
40
==PAGE:300==
8
Discussion
Certain remarks on assumptions and theories have been made in the previous chapters.
This chapter picks up on these remarks and discusses the validity of assumptions, what
might need more detailed modelling, the context in which diﬀerent transport theories
apply, and where simplifying assumptions might be reasonable in order to make the
models easier to solve or understand.
The models developed in this report increase in complexity almost exclusively by detail-
ing the changes happening down the tube, but each model relies on the same transport
mechanics to be true across the tube and assume those to be true through the entire
length of the tube. As a result, the models are only representative of systems where the
phases do not change so much as to change the transport phenomena between them.
Another very important factor for the mass and energy transport across the diﬀerent
phase contact interfaces is turbulence, which is not at all handled in this report. The
counter-current system is particularly prone to produce signiﬁcant turbulence.
The steady state axial ﬂow model omits pressure as a variable, assuming pressure to
change so little it may be approximated to a constant value. This assumption will be
better for short and wide tubes and worse for long and narrow tubes. Even though
the model avoids a description of geometry it does not mean the model is suited for
any given geometry, as exempliﬁed by the pressure simpliﬁcation. There are likely to
be other pitfalls related to the lack of geometrical deﬁnition, like what happens to the
vapour ﬂow as the liquid condensate takes up more space.
The axial model reverted the liquid ﬂux equation (3.2) to a more general form thereby
introducing two additional variables (xL
1 and xL
2 ). However, the results with variations
on this particular modiﬁcation (tested via the kL parameter) indicated that this level
of detail made only a small diﬀerence. It is worth considering whether the model (and
the following dynamic model) has other overly detailed contributions which add little
beneﬁt for the hassle. This partly reinforces the decision to not include T L as a variable.
Another consequence of this discovery is that speculations on proper modelling of the
liquid molar density, cL
t , may be completely irrelevant because it contributes to a term
with little impact.
The dynamic model introduces some description and thus dependence on the system
geometry in the form of assigning diﬀerent radii to the cylinders (see ﬁgure 6.1). The
radii are assumed constant, which may be a fair assumption on a diﬀerential level, but
over the entire length of the tube, the ﬂow of the liquid will increase thereby increasing
either the thickness of the liquid layer or the velocity of the ﬂow. The reality is likely
a combination. Increased ﬂow velocity could have implications too. This could poten-
tially change the mass and energy transfer coeﬃcients across interfaces with the liquid.
Before the dynamic model is solved, it is hard to guess at the resulting eﬀects of these
simpliﬁcations.
41
==PAGE:301==
Solving the dynamic model is discussed brieﬂy in chapter 7.
Just to reiterate, it is
my humble opinion that the system needs to be solved either with method of character-
istics, which is a fairly versatile analytical method, or discretised in both the time and
the area variables. Solving the system with counter-current ﬂow may require a program-
mer’s approach, where the vapour inlet (which is at the end point, area-wise) is found
by a loop, although, a mathematical solution would be preferable.
42
==PAGE:302==
9
Conclusion
Condensation of a methanol-water binary vapour mixture in a cooled tube has been
modelled with an increasing degree of complexity. The necessary theory for mass and
energy transport phenomena is presented in chapter 3 and the systems illustrated in
chapter 2.
The ﬁrst model utilises just the equations presented in chapter 3, combining the theory
to solve for a series of interface relevant variables (see table 9.1) given a known set of
variables for the vapour and the coolant (see table 9.2) and the parameter values from
table 4.3.
Table 9.1: Solved variables - same as table 4.1

The model was then expanded to describe what happens with the variables listed in
table 5.1, as they are changed down the area of the tube, as a result of the above calcu-
lated radial transport. A set of diﬀerential equations were added to the existing system
of equations. The system is best suited for solving co-current ﬂow, but a few changes -
easily available in the script - allows for some simulation of counter-current ﬂow, albeit
without deﬁnition of the actual vapour inlet (vapour is deﬁned at A = 0 which is the
vapour outlet), and without any modiﬁcations to the radial transport equations. Both
models produce believable results but the accuracy of the solutions cannot be veriﬁed as
no physical system was compared with the simulations. Results of the simulations can
be found in plots starting from page 25 and in appendix A.
43
==PAGE:303==
The model reacted predictably when exposed to variations in diﬀerent parameters, ex-
cept when it came to the value for mass transfer coeﬃcient from the interface to the bulk
liquid, kL. This was shown to have very little impact on the system as a whole. Besides
kL, two other parameters, T C and h0, were varied to get an idea of their importance
and to test the model. Decreasing the coolant temperature to 20◦C meant that 150 m2
of tube area condensed about the same amount of vapour as 400 m2 did with a coolant
temperature of 39.1◦C. This means the temperature diﬀerence (at the inlet) between the
vapour and the coolant was increased from 50.9◦C to 70◦C.
The highest level of model complexity was the addition of the dynamic behaviour of
the system, relevant for modelling of unsteady state for whatever purpose. This was
done by adding terms of accumulated material and energy for the existing material and
energy balances, respectively, which required control volumes to be deﬁned thus intro-
ducing a need for a more speciﬁed geometry. The model was developed for co-current
ﬂow, but the logic of developing diﬀerent ﬂow patterns is mentioned as well. The ﬁnal
equations were continuously tested to make sure that they simpliﬁed to the previous
model in the steady state. A few examples of this has been provided, but not all are
fully written out.
Two versions of the same model have been provided (section 6.3 and appendix B). The
ﬁrst focuses on breaking down the equations to forms that are equivalent to those given
in section 5.4, using the same variables, the same number of equations, and eliminating
elements that cannot be evaluated, in particular functions for enthalpy. The other set
is structured on divergent form which lends itself to certain types discretisation meth-
ods. It is important that these are on a form where density is in the time derivative,
ﬂux is in the space derivative, and the rest is isolated [2]. This structure may require
the solved variables to be redeﬁned in order that they ﬁt the new structure more directly.
The dynamic system is a system of both algebraic and partial equations with seven
or eight partial diﬀerential equations depending on what assumptions are made. It was
not solved, but a short discussion on how this might be accomplished has been provided.
Chapter 8 picks up on some interesting points, thoughts, and observations made dur-
ing the development process. Assumptions, simpliﬁcations, and modelling minutiae are
reﬂected upon and may inspire ideas for anyone who would be interested in continuing
this topic.
44
